The Mind and Infinite Prior Premises
Dialectical exchange on mental capacity and infinite premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange about the mind's capacity to hold infinite premises and explain why sequential preparatory knowledge suffices for certainty.

Core Idea
Preparatory knowledge need not coexist with the conclusion to produce certainty.

This passage tackles a common objection against the possibility of demonstration: if a proof requires an infinite number of prior premises, the mind cannot hold them all at once, so the proof fails. The answer distinguishes between preparatory mental motions and the final assent, showing that sequential acquisition is sufficient. We will dissect this dialectical exchange using the example of complex geometrical problems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the objector's claim: that the mind cannot encompass infinite things at once, so the proof fails. The passage begins with this objection as a premise.
2
Locate the answer's key distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'mental motions' (الحركات الفكرية) as preparatory (معدات) and the final sciences/perceptions themselves. Preparatory things cannot coexist with the result, but the sciences/perceptions might, yet they are not required to all coexist at once.
The author uses 'معدات' for preparations.
3
Examine the geometric example
Consider a geometry problem with many premises. After solving it, you become oblivious to distant premises and even proximate ones, yet you remain certain of the conclusion. This shows that premises need not be simultaneously present.
The passage says 'في المسائل الهندسية كثيرة المقدمات'.
4
Understand the final verdict
The author concludes that the objection is not fully refuted but requires the specific answer given by the commentator. The infinite things are considered 'preparatory' (معدات) or like them, so they do not need to exist together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ي إحاطة الذهن بأمور غير متناهية دفعة واحدة، وهو محال، فيتم الدليل ويسقط الاعتراض. وأجيب بأنه لاشك أن الحركات الفكرية معدات الحصول المطلوب، ممتنعة الاجتماع معه. وأما ما يقع فيه تلك المعدات - أعني العلوم والإدراكات - وإن لم يمتنع اجتماعها مع المطلوب، لكنّها ليست تما يجب اجتماعها بأسرها معه دفعة، فإنّا نجد من أنفسنا في القياسات المركبة الكثيرة المقدّمات والنتائج التي يتوصل بها إلى المطلوب، أنا نذهل عند حصول المطلوب عن كثير من تلك المقدمات السابقة مع الجزم بالمطلوب، بل ربما نغفل بعد ماحصل لنا المطلوب عن المقدمات القريبة التي بها حصل لنا المطلوب ابتداء مع ملاحظة المطلوب وحصوله بالفعل. وذلك ظاهر في المسائل الهندسية الكثيرة المقدمات جداً، فإن من زوالها علم أنه عند ماحصل له التصديق المطلوب بتلك المسائل قد ذهل عن المقدمات البعيدة ذهولاً تاماً بلا ارتياب في ذلك التصديق، وعلم أيضاً أنه يلاحظ تلك المسائل بعد حصولها ويجزم بها جزماً يقينياً مع الغفلة عن المقدمات القريبة أيضاً. نعم يعلم إجمالاً أن هناك مقدمات يقينية توجب اليقين بهذا التصديق. فظهر أن العلوم والإدراكات السابقة لا يجب اجتماعها مع المطلوب دفعة بل يكفي حصولها متعاقبة، وحينئذ كان ذلك الاعتراض متجهاً غير ساقط، ومحتاجاً إلى الجواب الذي ذكره الشارح. وإنما حكم على تلك الأمور غير المتناهية بكونها معدات لأنها محال المعدات، أو في حكمها في عدم لزوم الاجتماع في الوجود، وإن كانت ممتازة عن المعدات في جواز الاجتماع في الجملة.
The mind's encompassing of infinite things at once is impossible, so the proof is completed and the objection falls. And it was answered that there is no doubt that mental motions are preparatory for obtaining the sought, impossible to coexist with it. As for what those preparations consist in – namely the sciences and perceptions – even if their coexistence with the sought is not impossible, yet it is not necessary that all of them coexist with it at once. For we find in ourselves in compound syllogisms with many premises and conclusions by which one reaches the sought, that we become oblivious upon obtaining the sought to many of those previous premises while still being certain of the sought; indeed, perhaps after we have obtained the sought we forget even the proximate premises by which we originally obtained the sought while observing the sought and its actual occurrence. This is evident in geometrical problems with very many premises, for whoever studies them knows that when he obtains the desired assent to those problems, he becomes completely oblivious to the remote premises without any doubt about that assent, and he also knows that he observes those problems after obtaining them and affirms them with certainty while also being oblivious to the proximate premises. Yes, he knows summarily that there are certain premises that necessitate certainty in this assent. Thus it becomes clear that the previous sciences and perceptions do not have to coexist with the sought at once; rather their sequential occurrence suffices. And then that objection was directed and not dropped, and needed the answer that the commentator mentioned. And he judged those infinite things to be preparations because they are places of preparations, or in their status in not necessitating coexistence in existence, even though they are distinguished from preparations in the permissibility of coexistence in some respect.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. First, the objector claims that the mind cannot encompass infinite things at once, so the proof of something requiring infinite prior knowledge fails. The answer refutes this by explaining that mental processes are preparatory (muddāt) and need not be present simultaneously. The commentator gives an example from geometry: after solving a complex geometric problem, one forgets many of the intermediate premises but still holds the conclusion with certainty. Thus, sequential knowledge suffices. The passage then clarifies that the objection is not fully refuted but needs the specific answer given by the commentator. It also distinguishes between infinite things as preparations and their actual coexistence.

Key Terms
الحركات الفكرية
المعدات
الذهول
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not necessary for all previous premises to be simultaneously present when one arrives at a logical conclusion?
A) Because the mind cannot hold multiple ideas at once
B) Because the premises are preparatory and sequential, and one often forgets them while still being certain of the conclusion
C) Because the premises are false
D) Because the conclusion is self-evident

Key Takeaway
Preparatory knowledge (المعدات) does not require simultaneous presence with the conclusion; sequential acquisition suffices for certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Khamr is intoxicating. Therefore khamr is forbidden.' In a discussion, someone objects: 'You are assuming the first premise, but you cannot prove it without an infinite regress.' Using the lesson's distinction, explain why the objection does not undermine the certainty of the conclusion.
?

The Circularity Objection: How Logic Avoids Infinite Regress
Dialectical exchange on the foundation of logical knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection of circularity against learning logic and explain how the author resolves it by distinguishing self-evident and acquired parts.

Core Idea
Logic's self-evident core stops the regress before it starts.

A common objection to logic is that if it is the tool for all acquired knowledge, learning logic itself would require prior logical knowledge, leading to circularity or infinite regress. The author of our text presents this objection and then provides a crisp answer that preserves the coherence of the logical enterprise. In this lesson, you will walk through the dialectical exchange and see how the distinction between self-evident and acquired logical principles resolves the problem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
The objector claims that if all of logic is acquired (kasbi), then learning any part presupposes another logical law, leading to daur (circularity) or tasalsul (infinite regress). Find this in the text where the objection is introduced.
Look for the phrase 'لا يقال' and the argument that follows.
2
Locate the author's initial response
The author denies the premise that logic is entirely acquired. He claims that logic has self-evident (badihi) parts, like the first figure, and acquired parts derived from them.
The response begins with 'بل'.
3
Analyze the follow-up objection
The objector then asks: even the derivation from self-evident to acquired requires reasoning, which would need another law. This is introduced by 'فإن قيل'.
This shows a deeper layer of the dialectic.
4
Examine the final answer
The author replies that the reasoning itself is self-evident, so no further law is needed. The regress stops at self-evident principles.
The answer begins with 'قلنا'.
5
Summarize the resolution
Logic is a mixture: self-evident foundations (badihi) and acquired extensions (kasbi) that rest on those foundations.
This prevents both circularity and infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : لانسلّم لزوم الدور أو التسلسل ؛ وإنما يلزم لولم ينته الاكتساب إلى قانون بديهي - وهو ممنوع.
لأنا نقول: المنطق مجموع قوانين الاكتساب، فإذا فرضنا أن المنطق كسبي وحاولنا اكتساب قانون منها - والتقدير أن الاكتساب لا يتم إلا بالمنطق - فيتوقف اكتساب ذلك القانون على قانون آخر، وهو أيضاً كسي على ذلك التقدير، فالدور أو التسلسل لازم.
وتقرير الجواب : أن المنطق ليس بجميع الأجزاء بديهياً - وإلا لاستغني عن تعلمه - ولا بجميع أجزائه كسبياً - وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل كما ذكره المعترض - بل بعض أجزائه بديهي - كالشكل الأول - والبعض الآخر كسي - كباقي الأشكال - والبعض الكسبي إنما يستفاد من البعض البديهي، فلا يلزم الدور ولا التسلسل.
فإن قيل : استفادة البعض الكسبي من البعض البديهي إنما تكون بطريق النظر، فيحتاج في معرفة ذلك النظر إلى قانون آخر فيعود المحذور.
قلنا : ذلك النظر أيضاً بديهي، فالكسبي من المنطق مستفاد من البديهي منه بطريق بديهي، فلاحاجة إلى قانون آخر أصلاً.
It is not said: We do not accept the necessity of circularity or infinite regress; it would only be necessary if acquisition did not end with a self-evident law—and that is denied.
We say: Logic is the sum of laws of acquisition. If we assume that logic is acquired and we try to acquire a law from it—given that acquisition is only completed through logic—then acquiring that law depends on another law, which is also acquired on that assumption, so circularity or infinite regress is necessary.
The formulation of the answer: Logic is not entirely self-evident—otherwise there would be no need to learn it—nor entirely acquired—otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow as the objector mentioned—but some of its parts are self-evident—like the first figure—and other parts are acquired—like the remaining figures. The acquired part is only derived from the self-evident part, so circularity and infinite regress do not follow.
If it is said: Deriving the acquired part from the self-evident part is only through reasoning, and knowing that reasoning requires another law, so the problem returns.
We say: That reasoning is also self-evident, so the acquired part of logic is derived from its self-evident part through a self-evident path, and there is no need for another law at all.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and its answer concerning whether logic itself can be learned without circularity or infinite regress. The objector claims that if all logical knowledge were acquired, then learning any rule would presuppose another rule ad infinitum. The commentator responds by dividing logic into self-evident parts (e.g., the first figure of the syllogism) and acquired parts (e.g., other figures). The acquired parts are derived from the self-evident ones, stopping the regress. A further objection asks whether the derivation itself requires reasoning, which would need another law. The answer: that reasoning is also self-evident, so no new law is needed. This resolution shows that logic has a foundational, self-evident core.

Key Terms
الدور
التسلسل
بديهي
كسبي
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that circularity or infinite regress does not occur in the acquisition of logic?
A) Because all parts of logic are self-evident.
B) Because all parts of logic are acquired.
C) Because some parts are self-evident and others are derived from them.
D) Because logic is not needed for acquisition.

Key Takeaway
Logic avoids circularity by having a self-evident foundation (badihi) from which acquired (kasbi) rules are derived, stopping infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a self-evident (badihi) logical rule that you know. Explain why it is considered self-evident and not acquired. Then, give an example of an acquired (kasbi) rule from logic that depends on it. (Use Islamic examples; e.g., the first figure syllogism is self-evident; the rules of second figure are acquired and proven via first figure.)
?
Construct a brief dialectical exchange (objection and answer) about the foundation of knowledge in Islamic theology. For example, an objector might say: 'We cannot know anything about Allah through reason because reasoning is fallible.' Provide an answer using the author's strategy of self-evident premises.

Three Aspects of Assent Knowledge in Logic
Author enumerates the three aspects of assent knowledge that logic investigates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the three aspects of assent knowledge that logic investigates and explain what each aspect covers.

Core Idea
Logic studies assent knowledge in three layers: the argument, its premises as propositions, and premises as potential propositions.

In this passage, the author outlines the three subjects of logical investigation concerning assent knowledge. Understanding this tripartite structure helps you see how logic builds from propositions to arguments to proofs, clarifying the scope of each investigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three aspects
Find in the Arabic the three items listed: the reaching to the unknown (syllogism/induction/analogy), the proximate dependence (propositions), and the remote dependence (premises and conclusions as potential propositions).
The author uses 'one', 'second', 'third' to enumerate.
2
Distinguish proximate vs remote dependence
Proximate dependence means directly needed to reach the unknown (the actual premises used in an argument). Remote dependence refers to the premises and conclusions considered as propositions in themselves, which are in potentiality.
Premises and conclusions are counted as assent-information, not conceptual, because they involve a judgment.
3
Understand why premises are assent-information
The author clarifies that premises and conclusions are propositions in proximate potentiality, thus they belong to assent knowledge. This is in contrast to subject and predicate, which are concepts.
This distinction prevents confusion between conceptual and assent-based elements in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أحوال المعلومات التصديقية التي يبحث عنها في المنطق فثلاثة أيضا: أحدها الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي - يقينيا كان أو غير يقيني، جازما أو غير جازم - وذلك مباحث القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل التي هي أنواع الحجة. وثانيها ما يتوقف عليه الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي توقفا قريبا، وذلك مباحث القضايا. وثالثها ما يتوقف عليه الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي توقفا بعيدا – أي بواسطة، ككون المعلومات التصديقية مقدمات وتوالي، فإن المقدم والتالي قضيتان بالقوة القريبة من الفعل؛ فهما معدودان في المعلومات التصديقية دون التصورية، بخلاف الموضوع والمحمول فإنّهما من قبيل التصورات (شريف).
As for the states of assent-information investigated in logic, they are also three: One: the reaching to the unknown assent - whether certain or not certain, decisive or not decisive - and that is the topics of syllogism, induction, and analogy, which are types of proof. Second: that upon which the reaching to the unknown assent depends proximately, and that is the topics of propositions. Third: that upon which the reaching to the unknown assent depends remotely - i.e., through an intermediary - like the assent-information being premises and conclusions; for the premise and conclusion are two propositions in proximate potentiality; thus they are counted among assent-information, not conceptual information, unlike the subject and predicate which are from the category of concepts (Sharif).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the three aspects of assent knowledge investigated in logic. The first is the actual process of reaching an unknown assent, which may be certain or uncertain, decisive or non-decisive; this includes syllogism, induction, and analogy, all of which are types of proof (ḥujja). The second is the proximate dependence: the propositions (qaḍāyā) that serve as premises for arguments. The third is the remote dependence: the premises and conclusions themselves, which are propositions in potentiality. Note that premises and conclusions are counted as assent-information, not conceptual, because they involve judgment, unlike subject and predicate which are concepts.

Key Terms
الحجة
مقدمات وتوالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are premises and conclusions counted as assent-information rather than conceptual?
A) Because they are certain.
B) Because they are propositions in potentiality.
C) Because they are actual propositions.
D) Because they are subjects and predicates.

Key Takeaway
Logic divides its study of assent knowledge into three categories: the argument itself (syllogism, induction, analogy), the propositions that serve as premises, and the premises and conclusions as propositions in potentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

Testing the Syllogism for Tadammun and Iltizam
Author states entailment and critiques syllogistic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the categorical syllogism that attempts to prove tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa.

Core Idea
A dependent (tabi') must be qualified by 'inasmuch as' to avoid false entailment.

This passage examines the claim that tadammun and iltizam necessarily entail mutabaqa because they are dependent on it. The author reconstructs the syllogism and then rigorously critiques its logical structure, focusing on the role of the 'inasmuch as' qualification (haythiyya) and the repetition of the middle term. Understanding this critique will sharpen your ability to evaluate syllogisms in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asserted conclusion
The passage begins by stating that tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa. Locate this claim and note the reason given: they are dependent (tabi') on mutabaqa.
The dependency relation is the core of the argument.
2
Reconstruct the syllogism
Extract the premises: Major premise: The dependent inasmuch as it is dependent does not exist without the depended-upon. Minor premise: Tadammun and iltizam are dependent inasmuch as they are dependent. Conclusion: They do not exist without mutabaqa.
Notice the qualification 'inasmuch as' (haythiyya) is critical.
3
Check middle term repetition
Identify the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). In the major premise, it is 'dependent (inasmuch as it is dependent)'; in the minor, it must be exactly the same. Determine if the qualification is applied consistently.
If the minor does not include 'inasmuch as', the middle term differs.
4
Assess the two-case critique
The author argues: if the minor qualifies with 'inasmuch as', the premise is deniable (tadammun is not 'dependent inasmuch as it is dependent' but simply an instance); if not, the middle term is not repeated and the syllogism fails.
This shows the syllogism is invalid either way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما هما - أي التضمّن والالتزام - فيستلزمان المطابقة، لأنهما لا يوجدان إلا معها، لأنهما تابعان لها والتابع من حيث أنه تابع لا يوجد بدون المتبوع، وإنما قيد بالحيثية احترازاً عن التابع الأعم كالحرارة للنار، فإنها تابعة للنار وقد توجد بدونها، كما في الشمس والحركة. وأما من حيث أنها تابعة للنار فلا توجد إلا معها. وفي هذا البيان نظر، لأن التابع في الصغرى إن قيد بالحيثية منعناها، وإن لم يقيد بها لم يتكرّر الحد الأوسط، فلا ينتج المطلوب. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأنّ الحيثية في الكبرى ليست قيداً للأوسط، بل معنى مركب فيكون التضمّن مستلزماً للالتزام، وهو باطل؛ لأنا قد نتصور معنى مركباً مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً وعن مفهوم الكلية والجزئية، فليس شيء منها لازماً ذهنياً يلزم من تصوّر الملزوم تصوّره، وقد يدعى هاهنا أيضاً أنا نجزم بجواز تعقل بعض المعاني المركبة مع الغفلة عن جميع المفهومات الخارجية، على قياس ماقيل في المطابقة، فلايكون التضمن مستلزما للالتزام (شريف). (١) وذلك لأنك إذا قلت : «التضمّن تابع من حيث هو تابع» فإن أردت أن التضمن نفس مفهوم التابع - كما يفهم من هذه العبارة – كان كاذباً قطعاً، لأن التضمن فرد من أفراد التابع - لانفس مفهومه – وإن أردت معنى آخر فلابد من تصويره حتى يتكلم عليه (شريف).
As for the two—i.e., inclusion and implication—they entail conformity, because they do not exist except with it, for they are dependent on it, and the dependent inasmuch as it is dependent does not exist without the depended-upon. And it is qualified by the aspect to guard against the more general dependent, like heat for fire, for it is dependent on fire and may exist without it, as in the sun and movement. But inasmuch as it is dependent on fire, it does not exist except with it. In this exposition there is a consideration, because the dependent in the minor premise if qualified by the aspect we deny it, and if not qualified by it, the middle term is not repeated, so the required does not result. And it can be answered that the aspect in the major is not a qualification of the middle, but rather a composite meaning, then inclusion would entail implication, which is false; for we may conceive a composite meaning while heedless of its being composite and of the concept of universality and particularity, so none of them is a mental necessary concomitant such that the conception of the entailed necessitates its conception. And it might also be claimed here that we are certain of the possibility of understanding some composite meanings while heedless of all external concepts, analogous to what was said about conformity, so inclusion does not entail implication (Sharif). (1) That is because if you say: 'Inclusion is a dependent inasmuch as it is dependent', if you mean that inclusion is the very concept of dependent—as understood from this expression—it is absolutely false, because inclusion is an instance of dependent, not its concept. And if you mean another meaning, it must be depicted so that it can be discussed (Sharif).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage refines the logical relationship between the three types of signification. The author asserts that tadammun and iltizam both entail mutabaqa, since they are dependent (tabi') on mutabaqa and cannot exist without it. To avoid counterexamples like heat depending on fire but also existing elsewhere, the argument introduces a qualification: 'inasmuch as it is dependent' (min haythu huwa tabi'). The critique points out a flaw: if the minor premise qualifies the middle term with this aspect, the syllogism fails; if not, the middle term is not repeated and the conclusion does not follow. A proposed answer suggests that the aspect is part of a composite meaning, but this leads to another problem: it would make tadammun entail iltizam, which is false. The marginal note by Sharif further dissects the language: saying 'inclusion is a dependent inasmuch as it is dependent' is ambiguous—it could mean inclusion is identical to the concept of dependent (false) or something else requiring clarification. This passage demonstrates the high standard of logical precision in assessing arguments about the nature of signification.

Key Terms
التابع
الحيثية
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw exposed in the reasoning that tadammun and iltizam entail mutabaqa?
A) The minor premise is false because tadammun is not dependent on mutabaqa.
B) The middle term is not properly repeated if the aspect qualification is omitted.
C) The major premise is false because iltizam can exist without mutabaqa.
D) The conclusion is too weak and does not follow.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism about dependency must have the middle term exactly repeated, and qualifications like 'inasmuch as' must be applied consistently to avoid false premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism that attempts to prove that the term 'zina' (adultery) entails legal punishment via tadammun, then test its validity by checking middle term repetition and the 'inasmuch as' qualification.
?
Identify the fallacy in a similar syllogism from a kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Check if the middle term is properly repeated.

Classifying Conditional Propositions by Composition
Classification of conditional propositions by composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional propositions into nine conjunctive and six disjunctive types based on their internal composition and distinguish between natural order (conjunctives) and conventional order (disjunctives).

Core Idea
Conjunctive conditionals have a natural order; disjunctives have only a conventional one — this determines their subtypes.

In earlier lessons, you learned that conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) come in two main forms: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). Now we delve deeper into how these conditionals are themselves constructed from component propositions. The author explains that depending on whether the components are categorical, conjunctive, or disjunctive, the total number of distinct conditional types changes—and crucially, the natural order of cause and effect in conjunctives forces more subtypes than the purely conventional order in disjunctives. Mastering this classification is essential for correctly analyzing conditional syllogisms in later logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
List the six possible compositions
A conditional is composed of two propositions, each of which is either categorical (hamliyya), conjunctive (muttasila), or disjunctive (munfasila). That yields six combinations: two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, categorical+conjunctive, categorical+disjunctive, and conjunctive+disjunctive.
The author states these are all the possibilities; no more.
2
Distinguish natural vs. conventional order
In a conjunctive conditional, the antecedent is naturally the entailing cause (malzum) and the consequent is the entailed effect (lazim). This order is fixed by meaning. In a disjunctive, the parts are opposites (mu'and) with symmetric opposition, so the assignment of antecedent/consequent is purely conventional (by wad').
This difference is the key to understanding the counting.
3
Apply the distinction to mixed compositions
For a conjunctive composed of a categorical and a conjunctive, there are two subtypes: one where the categorical is the antecedent and the conjunctive the consequent, and another with reversed order. These are logically distinct because the natural order breaks symmetry. For a disjunctive composed of the same, the two orders are logically equivalent, so only one subtype exists.
The same reasoning applies to the other mixed pairs: categorical+disjunctive and conjunctive+disjunctive.
4
Count the total types
Conjunctives: three pure compositions (1 each) = 3; three mixed compositions × 2 subtypes each = 6; total 9. Disjunctives: three pure compositions = 3; three mixed compositions × 1 subtype each = 3; total 6.
The author gives examples for the first three conjunctive types; you are encouraged to derive the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والشرطية قد تتركب عن حمليتين وعن متصلتين وعن منفصلتين وعن حملية ومتصلة وعن حملية ومنفصلة وعن متصلة ومنفصلة، وكل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة في المتصلة تنقسم إلى قسمين لامتياز مقدمها عن تاليها بالطبع بخلاف المنفصلة، فإنّ مقدّمها إنما يتميز عن تاليها بالوضع فقط، فأقسام المتصلات تسعة والمنفصلات ستة. وأما الأمثلة فعليك باستخراجها من نفسك. أقول: لما كانت الشرطيّة مركبة من قضيتين والقضية إما حملية أو متصلة أو منفصلة، كان تركيبها إما من حمليتين أو متصلتين أو منفصلتين أو من حملية ومتصلة أو حمليّة ومنفصلة أو منفصلة ومتصلة لاتزيد على هذه الأقسام، لكن كلّ واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة الأخيرة ينقسم في المتصلة إلى قسمين، لأن مقدّم المتصلة متميّز عن تاليها بحسب الطبع : أي بحسب المفهوم، فإن مفهوم المقدّم فيها الملزوم، ومفهوم التالي فيها اللازم، ويحتمل أن يكون الشيء ملزوماً لآخر ولا يكون لازما له، فالمقدم في المتصلة متعيّن لأن يكون مقدّماً والتالي متعين لأن يكون تالياً، بخلاف المنفصلة فإن مفهوم التالي فيها المعاند ومفهوم المقدم المعاند، والمعاند لابد أن يكون معائداً أيضاً، لأن عناد أحد الشيئين للآخر في قوة عناد الآخر له، فحال كل واحد من جزأيها عند الآخر حال واحدة، وإنما عرض لأحدهما أن يكون مقدماً وللآخر أن يكون تالياً بمجرد الوضع - لا الطبع - ففرق بين المتصلة المركبة من الحمليّة والمتصلة والمقدّم فيها الحملية، وبينها والمقدّم فيها المتصلة؛ بخلاف المنفصلة المركبة منهما فلافرق بينهما إذا كان المقدّم فيها الحمليّة أو المتصلة، وكذلك في المركبة من الحملية والمنفصلة ومن المتصلة والمنفصلة؛ فلاجرم انقسمت الأقسام الثلاثة في المتصلة إلى قسمين دون المنفصلة. فأقسام المتصلات تسعة، وأقسام المنفصلات ستة. أما أمثلة المتصلات : فالأول من حمليتين، كقولك : كلّما كان الشيء إنساناً فهو حيوان». والثاني من متصلتين، كقولنا : كلّما كان الشيء إنساناً فهو حيوان، فكلما لم يكن الشيء حيواناً لم يكن إنساناً». والثالث من منفصلتين، كقولنا : كلّما كان دائماً إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً ، فدائماً إما أن يكون منقسماً بمتساويين أو غير منقسم».
He said: The conditional may be composed of two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical and a conjunctive, a categorical and a disjunctive, and a conjunctive and a disjunctive. Each of these last three, in the conjunctive, divides into two types because its antecedent is distinguished from its consequent naturally, unlike the disjunctive, for its antecedent is distinguished from its consequent only by convention. So the types of conjunctives are nine and the types of disjunctives are six. As for examples, you must derive them yourself. I say: Since the conditional is composed of two propositions, and the proposition is either categorical, conjunctive, or disjunctive, its composition is necessarily from two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical and a conjunctive, a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive—no more than these types. But each of the last three types, in the conjunctive, divides into two types, because the antecedent of the conjunctive is distinguished from its consequent by nature, i.e., according to concept. For the concept of the antecedent in it is the entailing (malzum), and the concept of the consequent is the entailed (lazim), and it is possible that a thing be an entailing of another without being entailed by it. So the antecedent in the conjunctive is determined to be antecedent and the consequent is determined to be consequent, unlike the disjunctive, for the concept of the consequent in it is the opposite (mu'and) and the concept of the antecedent is the opposite, and the opposite must also be opposite to the other, because the opposition of one of two things to the other is equivalent to the opposition of the other to it. So the status of each of its two parts with respect to the other is the same, and it happens that one of them is deemed antecedent and the other consequent merely by convention—not by nature. So there is a difference between the conjunctive composed of a categorical and a conjunctive with the categorical as antecedent and the one with the conjunctive as antecedent; unlike the disjunctive composed of them, for there is no difference between them whether the antecedent is the categorical or the conjunctive. Similarly for the composition of a categorical and a disjunctive, and of a conjunctive and a disjunctive. Hence the three types in the conjunctive necessarily divide into two, but not in the disjunctive. So the types of conjunctives are nine, and the types of disjunctives are six. As for examples of conjunctives: the first is from two categoricals, like your saying: 'Whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal.' The second is from two conjunctives, like our saying: 'Whenever a thing is a human, it is an animal, so whenever a thing is not an animal, it is not a human.' The third is from two disjunctives, like our saying: 'Whenever it is always either that this number is even or odd, then it is always either that it is divisible into two equals or not divisible.'
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the classification of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) based on their internal composition. The author begins by listing the possible compositions: from two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, or mixed pairs (categorical+conjunctive, categorical+disjunctive, conjunctive+disjunctive). He then explains a key distinction: when a conjunctive conditional is composed from the last three mixed types, each of those types splits into two subtypes because in a conjunctive, the antecedent and consequent have a natural order (the antecedent is the entailing cause, malzum; the consequent is the entailed effect, lazim). This natural order means that swapping the antecedent and consequent changes the meaning, so we must distinguish cases where the categorical is antecedent vs. where the conjunctive is antecedent, etc. In contrast, a disjunctive conditional has no such natural order—both parts are opposites (mu'and) of each other, and the opposition is mutual, so the assignment of antecedent and consequent is purely conventional (by wad'). Therefore, for disjunctives, there is no distinction between the two orderings; they count as one type. The author then concludes that the total number of conjunctive types is nine, and disjunctive types is six. He encourages the student to derive examples themselves, but then provides three illustrative examples for conjunctive conditionals: the first from two categoricals, the second from two conjunctives, and the third from two disjunctives.

Key Terms
الشرطية
المتصلة
المنفصلة
المقدم
التالي
الحملية

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does a conjunctive conditional composed of a categorical and a conjunctive divide into two types, while a disjunctive conditional composed of the same does not?
A) Because the conjunctive has a natural order of antecedent and consequent, while the disjunctive has only a conventional order.
B) Because the conjunctive has two parts, while the disjunctive has only one.
C) Because the categorical and conjunctive cannot both appear in a disjunctive.
D) Because the author did not provide examples for the disjunctive.

Key Takeaway
Conjunctive conditionals have a natural order (malzum then lazim), so swapping changes meaning; disjunctives have mutual opposition, so order is conventional and does not affect logical type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conjunctive conditional from a categorical proposition and a conjunctive proposition, writing two versions: one where the categorical is the antecedent, and one where the conjunctive is the antecedent. Explain why they are distinct according to the natural order principle.
?
Consider the disjunctive conditional: 'Either the prayer is valid or it is invalid.' Explain why reversing the order to 'Either the prayer is invalid or it is valid' yields the same logical type, and why the distinction between the two orders is unnecessary in disjunctives.

Refutation of Conversion Proofs for Possible Propositions
Author refutes two methods for proving conversion of possible propositions: iftirad and aks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two flawed methods used to prove conversion of possible propositions and explain why each fails using the author’s critique.

Core Idea
A possible proposition's conversion cannot be proven by hypothetical syllogism or simple conversion.

The logicians attempted to prove that 'some A is possibly B' implies 'some B is possibly A' using two methods: hypothesis (iftirad) and conversion (aks). However, the author shows both are invalid. Understanding why they fail sharpens your ability to evaluate modal arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Locate the descriptions of iftirad and aks in the Arabic passage. Iftirad posits an actual individual that satisfies two descriptions to derive the conversion. Aks tries to prove by reductio, assuming the conversion false leads to a necessary negative.
The passage calls them the second and third proofs.
2
Reconstruct the iftirad syllogism
The iftirad method depends on a categorical syllogism with a possible minor premise in the first or third figure. Identify the premises and try to construct the argument in your mind.
The author says such syllogisms are 'sterile' (ʿaqima) because a possible minor premise does not produce a necessary conclusion.
3
Reconstruct the aks reductio
The aks method assumes the falsity of the conversion, which implies a necessary universal negative. That negative would then convert, but the author notes the conversion of a necessary negative only yields a permanent (da’ima) proposition, not a necessary one, breaking the reductio.
The contradiction sought requires a necessary negative, but only a permanent one is available.
4
Analyze the author's refutation
Read the passage from 'These proofs are not complete' onward. The first two fail because of unproductive possible minors; the third fails because necessary negatives do not convert as necessary.
The author’s final position is tawaqquf (suspension of judgment).
5
Apply the critique to similar arguments
Test other modal conversion claims using the same reasoning: check if the proof relies on a sterile syllogism or an invalid conversion step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : الافتراض، وهو أن يفرض ذات (ج و ب) (د) فرد ب) بالإمكان، و(دج) ، فبعض (ب ج) بالإمكان وهو المطلوب. وثالثها : طريق العكس، فإنه لو كذب بعض (ب ج)» بالإمكان لصدق «لاشيء من (ب) (ج) بالضرورة»، فينعكس إلى «لاشيء من (ج ب بالضرورة»، وقد كان بعض (ج) (ب) بالإمكان» فيجتمع النقيضان. وهذه الدلائل لا تتم : أمّا الأوّلان : فلتوقفهما على انتاج الصغرى الممكنة في الشكل الأول والثالث - وستعرف أنها عقيمة – وأما الثالث : فلتوقفه على انعكاس السالبة الضرورية كنفسها، وقد تبين أنها لا تنعكس إلا دائمة، فلما لم تتمّ هذه الدلائل ولم يظفر المصنف بدليل يدلّ على الانعكاس ولا على عدمه توقف فيه.
And the second of them: hypothesis (iftirad), which is to posit a thing that is (J and B?) (D?) individual B with possibility, and D? J, so some B J with possibility, and that is the desired. And the third of them: the method of conversion (aks): for if 'some B J with possibility' were false, then 'nothing of B is J with necessity' would be true, which converts to 'nothing of J is B with necessity', while 'some J is B with possibility' was present, so the two contradictories would combine. These proofs are not complete: as for the first two, because they depend on the productivity of the possible minor premise in the first and third figures—and you will know that they are sterile—and as for the third, because it depends on the conversion of the necessary negative as itself, and it has been shown that it converts only as permanent. Since these proofs are not complete and the author did not find a proof indicating conversion nor its absence, he stopped (tawaqqafa) concerning it.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage critically evaluates two proposed methods for proving the conversion of possible propositions, i.e., that 'Some A is B with possibility' implies 'Some B is A with possibility'. The first method is al-iftirad (hypothesis), which assumes an actual individual that is both J and possibly B to derive the desired conversion. The second method is al-‘aks (conversion proper), which attempts to derive the conversion by reductio: if the conversion were false, then a necessary negative would hold, which after conversion would contradict the original possible. The author then explains why both fail: the first two (iftirad and a similar method) rely on the productivity of a possible minor premise in the first and third figures of the categorical syllogism, which is known to be unproductive ('aqima). The third method relies on the conversion of a necessary universal negative as itself, but it has been shown that such a conversion yields only a 'permanent' (da’ima) proposition, not a necessary one. Consequently, the author declares that he stopped (tawaqqafa) and did not affirm the conversion of possible propositions, following the position of the text being commented upon.

Key Terms
الافتراض (al-iftirāḍ)
العكس (al-‘aks)
الممكنة (al-mumkina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the third method (aks) fail to prove the conversion of possible propositions?
A) Because it assumes a false contradictory
B) Because it depends on the conversion of a necessary negative as itself, which is not valid
C) Because the possible minor premise cannot be used in the first figure
D) Because the reductio argument is circular

Key Takeaway
Possible propositions do not convert via iftirad or simple aks; the refutation hinges on sterile syllogisms and invalid conversion of necessary negatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Universal Affirmative Conditional Entails Two Disjunctives
Author explains how a universal affirmative conditional implies two disjunctives and vice versa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the two disjunctives entailed by any universal affirmative conditional and verify their converse relationship.

Core Idea
Every universal conditional implies two disjunctives, and they imply it back.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a key logical equivalence: a universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'Whenever A, then B') entails not one but two distinct disjunctives—one that prevents the coexistence of A and not-B, and another that prevents the absence of both not-A and B. Moreover, these disjunctives are converse: they also entail the original conditional. Understanding this relationship is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology that rely on conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal affirmative conditional
Find a conditional statement in the form 'Whenever A, B' in the passage. Note that it is called 'al-muttasila al-mujaba al-kulliyya'.
The passage uses terms like 'عين المقدم' (the very antecedent) and 'نقيض التالي' (negation of consequent).
2
Extract the two disjunctives
The first disjunctive is man' al-jam' (preventing combination) between A and not-B. The second is man' al-khuluw (preventing emptiness) between not-A and B. Write them out symbolically.
These correspond to exclusive and inclusive disjunctions respectively.
3
Prove the entailment from conditional to disjunctives
Using reduction (khalaf): if the disjunctive were false, the conditional would fail. For example, if man' al-jam' were false, then A and not-B could coexist, contradicting the conditional.
The passage provides two reductions for each disjunctive.
4
Prove the converse: disjunctives entail the conditional
Similarly, if the disjunctive were false, the conditional would fail. For man' al-jam', if the conditional were false, A and not-B could coexist, violating the disjunctive.
This shows mutual entailment (converse).
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take a conditional from fiqh or kalam, e.g., 'If the sale is valid, ownership transfers,' and derive the two disjunctives.
Use the same reduction to verify the relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الرابع في تلازم الشرطيات أما المتصلة الموجبة الكلّية فتستلزم منفصلة مانعة الجمع من عين المقدم ونقيض التالي، ومانعة الخلو من نقيض المقدّم وعين التالي متعاكسين عليها، وإلا لبطل اللزوم والاتصال. القول : المراد بالمتصلة في هذا الباب أعني باب تلازم الشرطيات : اللزومية، وبالمنفصلة : العنادية، فمتى صدق اللزوم الكلي بين أمرين منع الجمع بين عين الملزوم ونقيض اللازم ومنع الخلو بين نقيض الملزوم وعين اللازم؛ وهذان الانفصالان متعاكسان على اللزوم - أي متى تحقق منع الجمع بين أمرين يكون عين كلّ واحد منهما مستلزماً لنقيض الآخر، ومتى تحقق منع الخلو بين أمرين يكون نقيض كل واحد منهما مستلزماً لعين الآخر. وأما أنّ اللزوم بين الأمرين يستلزم الانفصالين فلأنه لولا ذلك لبطل اللزوم بينهما، فإنّه على تقدير اللزوم بين أمرين لولم يصدق منع الجمع بين عين الملزوم ونقيض اللازم لجاز ثبوت الملزوم مع نقيض اللازم فيجوز وقوع الملزوم بدون اللازم، فيبطل الملازمة بينهما - هذا خلف. وكذلك لو لم يصدق منع الخلو بين نقيض الملزوم وعين اللازم لجاز ارتفاع نقيض الملزوم وعين اللازم فيجوز ثبوت الملزوم بدون اللازم، فيبطل اللزوم بينهما - هذا خلف . وأما أن الانفصالين متعاكسان على اللزوم : فلأنه لولاه لبطل الانفصال فإنّه إذا تحقق منع الجمع بين أمرين فلو لم يجب ثبوت نقيض الآخر على تقدير عين كل واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت عين الآخر على ذلك التقدير، فيجوز اجتماع العينين فلايكون بينهما منع الجمع، وكذلك إذا تحقق منع الخلو بين أمرين، فلولم يجب ثبوت عين الآخر على تقدير نقيض كل واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت نقيض الآخر على ذلك التقدير فيجوز ارتفاعهما، فلايكون بينهما منع الخلو.
The fourth research on the interrelations of conditionals. As for the universal affirmative conditional, it entails a disjunctive that prevents combination (man' al-jam') from the very antecedent and the negation of the consequent, and a disjunctive that prevents emptiness (man' al-khuluw) from the negation of the antecedent and the very consequent, and they are converse upon it; otherwise the implication and connection would be invalid. The statement: what is meant by 'conditional' in this chapter, i.e., the chapter on interrelations of conditionals, is the implicative (luzumiyya), and by 'disjunctive' is the adversative ('inadiyya). So whenever universal implication holds between two things, it prevents combination between the very implied term (malzum) and the negation of the necessary term (lazim), and prevents emptiness between the negation of the malzum and the very lazim. And these two disjunctives are converse upon the implication - i.e., whenever the prevention of combination between two things is realized, each of them implies the negation of the other; and whenever the prevention of emptiness between two things is realized, the negation of each implies the other. As for the fact that the implication between two things entails the two disjunctives: it is because if it were not so, the implication between them would be invalid. For if implication holds between two things, and the prevention of combination between the very malzum and the negation of lazim were not true, then it would be possible for the malzum to exist along with the negation of lazim, so it would be possible for the malzum to occur without the lazim, thus invalidating the implication - this is a reduction (khalaf). Similarly, if the prevention of emptiness between the negation of malzum and the very lazim were not true, then it would be possible for both the negation of malzum and the very lazim to be absent, so it would be possible for the malzum to exist without the lazim, thus invalidating the implication - this is a reduction. As for the fact that the two disjunctives are converse upon the implication: it is because if it were not so, the disjunctive would be invalid. For if prevention of combination between two things is realized, and it is not necessary that the negation of the other holds given the very existence of each, then it would be possible for the other to exist in that case, so both would coexist, and there would be no prevention of combination between them. Similarly, if prevention of emptiness between two things is realized, and it is not necessary that the other holds given the negation of each, then it would be possible for the negation of the other to exist in that case, so both would be absent, and there would be no prevention of emptiness.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between a universal affirmative conditional and two types of disjunctive propositions. The author first states that such a conditional entails a disjunctive that prevents the combination of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent (مانعة الجمع), and another disjunctive that prevents the emptiness of the negation of the antecedent and the consequent (مانعة الخلو). Furthermore, these two disjunctives are converse (متعاكسان) upon the conditional, meaning they also imply the original conditional. The commentator explains that here 'conditional' means the implicative type (لزومية), and 'disjunctive' means the adversative type (عنادية). The proof of the entailment from conditional to disjunctives is by reduction: if either disjunctive were false, then the conditional would be false because the antecedent could occur without the consequent. The proof of the converse (disjunctives implying the conditional) is also by reduction: if the conjunction of the antecedent and negation of consequent were possible, then the prevention of combination would be false; similarly, if the absence of both antecedent and consequent were possible, then prevention of emptiness would be false. This is a standard exposition of the interdefinability of conditional and disjunctive propositions in classical logic.

Key Terms
المتصلة الموجبة الكلية
منفصلة مانعة الجمع
منفصلة مانعة الخلو
متعاكسين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a universal affirmative conditional entails:
A) Only a disjunctive that prevents combination (man' al-jam').
B) Only a disjunctive that prevents emptiness (man' al-khuluw).
C) Both types of disjunctives, and they are converse to the conditional.
D) No disjunctive at all.

Key Takeaway
A universal affirmative conditional is logically equivalent to two disjunctives: one preventing the combination of antecedent and negation of consequent, and the other preventing the absence of both the negation of antecedent and the consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional 'Whenever a prayer is invalid, it must be repeated.' Derive the two disjunctives as the passage describes.
?
Verify the converse: show that the two disjunctives together entail the original conditional.

The Syllogism

10 min
True Disjunctive Entails Four Conditionals
Author explains how a true disjunctive entails four conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the four conditional propositions entailed by any true (exclusive) disjunction and verify the entailment using reduction.

Core Idea
An exclusive disjunction logically implies four conditionals: each part negates the other, and each negation affirms the other.

In this passage, the author explains that a true disjunctive (منفصلة حقيقية) — which excludes both co-truth and co-falsity — entails four conditional statements. Understanding this entailment is essential for analysing arguments that involve exclusive alternatives, such as in legal reasoning about mutually exclusive categories. We will walk through the derivation and proof.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the two parts of the disjunction
Identify the two mutually exclusive and exhaustive parts, e.g., A and B. In Islamic legal terms, these could be 'wajib' and 'mubah' for an act.
The disjunction is exclusive: both cannot be true, and both cannot be false.
2
State the first two conditionals
Each part implies the negation of the other: If A then not B; if B then not A. This follows from the exclusivity preventing both being true.
These are called 'مقدم متصلتين عين أحد الجزأين وتاليهما نقيض الآخر' in the text.
3
State the other two conditionals
The negation of each part implies the other: If not A then B; if not B then A. This follows from the exhaustiveness preventing both being false.
These are the 'مقدم أخريين نقيض أحد الجزأين وتاليهما عين الآخر'.
4
Prove one direction by reduction (خلف)
Assume the antecedent of one conditional is true but the consequent false. For example, if A is true but not B is false (i.e., B is true), then both A and B are true, contradicting the exclusivity. Therefore the conditional must hold.
Similar proofs apply to the other three conditionals.
5
Extend the proof to all four
By symmetry, each of the four conditionals is necessary. The text provides the proof for the first; the others follow analogously.
The entailment is a logical equivalence: the disjunction is true iff all four conditionals are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنفصلة الحقيقية تستلزم أربع متصلات مقدّم الاثنين عين أحد الجزأين وتاليهما نقيض الآخر ومقدّم الآخرين نقيض أحد الجزأين وتاليهما عين الآخر الجزأين. القول : والمنفصلة الحقيقية تستلزم أربع متصلات : مقدم متصلتين عين أحد الجزأين، وتاليهما نقيض الآخر، ومقدّم أخريين نقيض أحد الجزأين، وتاليهما عين الآخر ، أي متى صدق الانفصال الحقيقي بين أمرين استلزم عين كل واحد منهما نقيض الآخر، ونقيض كل واحد منهما عين الآخر. وأما الأوّل فلانه لولم يجب ثبوت نقيض الآخر على تقدير عين كلّ واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت عين الآخر على ذلك التقدير فيجوز اجتماعهما، وكان بينهما انفصال حقيقي - هذا خلف.
And the true disjunctive entails four conditionals: the antecedent of two of them is the very one of the two parts, and their consequent is the negation of the other; and the antecedent of the other two is the negation of one of the two parts, and their consequent is the very other of the two parts. The statement: The true disjunctive entails four conditionals: the antecedent of two conditionals is the very one of the two parts, and their consequent is the negation of the other; and the antecedent of the other two is the negation of one of the two parts, and their consequent is the very other, meaning: whenever a true disjunction holds between two things, it entails that the very existence of each implies the negation of the other, and the negation of each implies the very existence of the other. As for the first: because if it were not necessary that the negation of the other holds given the very existence of each, then it would be possible for the other to exist in that case, so both would coexist, and there would be a true disjunction between them - this is a reduction [contradiction].
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage discusses the entailments of a true disjunctive (المنفصلة الحقيقية), which is an exclusive disjunction that allows neither both true nor both false. The author states that such a disjunction entails four conditional propositions. In the first two conditionals, the antecedent is one part and the consequent is the negation of the other part. In the other two conditionals, the antecedent is the negation of one part and the consequent is the other part. The commentator explains that this means: if the disjunction is true, then each part implies the negation of the other (so they cannot both be true), and the negation of each implies the other (so they cannot both be false). The proof for the first of these (the implication from one part to the negation of the other) is by reduction: if the negation did not follow, then the other part could be true, leading to both being true, which contradicts the exclusivity of the disjunction. The text then pauses, presumably the proofs for the other three conditionals are similar (by reduction). This shows the interdefinability between exclusive disjunction and these four conditionals.

متصلات
مقدم

نقيض

Key Takeaway
A true exclusive disjunction is interdefinable with four conditional statements: each part implies the other's negation and each negation implies the other part.

?

?

Implication and Disjunction: Interdefinability in Post-Avicennian Logic
Author proves mutual entailment between implication and disjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that implication (luzum) entails both prevention of combination (man' al-jam') and prevention of void (man' al-khuluw), and that each disjunction entails implication, using proofs by contradiction.

Core Idea
Implication and disjunction are two sides of the same logical coin.

This passage from a commentary on the Shamsiyya rigorously demonstrates the deep logical equivalence between conditional implication and the two fundamental types of disjunction: exclusive (man' al-jam') and inclusive (man' al-khuluw). By walking through these proofs, you will sharpen your ability to transform conditional statements into disjunctive forms and vice versa—a crucial skill for analyzing complex arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define the three logical relations
Identify the definitions in the passage: implication (luzum) means 'if A then B' (A entails B); prevention of combination (man' al-jam') means A and B cannot both be true; prevention of void (man' al-khuluw) means A and B cannot both be false. Note that the author focuses on specific pairs: (entailed B, negation of entailer ¬A) for man' al-jam' and (negation of entailed ¬B, entailer A) for man' al-khuluw.
These are not the only pairs; the author chooses them strategically for the proof.
2
Trace implication → man' al-jam'
Assume A→B holds. If man' al-jam' between B and ¬A were false, then B and ¬A could coexist, i.e., B true with A false. But that contradicts A→B (since if A is false, B could still be true, but the implication only requires that when A is true, B is true; however, the coexistence of B and ¬A allows B true without A, which is allowed? Actually the author argues: if B and ¬A can coexist, then we can have B true while A false, which means B can occur without A, violating the entailment? Wait, entailment is usually defined as: A→B means it is impossible for A to be true and B false. So if B and ¬A are both true, then A is false, so A→B is vacuously true. That's not a contradiction. The author's reasoning is different: he says 'the entailed could occur without the entailer', which breaks the entailment. In classical Arabic logic, luzum means that if the entailer is true, the entailed must be true; but if the entailer is false, nothing follows. So the coexistence of B and ¬A does not directly break A→B. However, the author seems to be using a stronger notion: perhaps that A and B are necessarily connected, so that B cannot be true without A being true? Actually from the passage: 'if prevention of combination between the same as the entailed and the contradictory of the entailer were not true, then it would be permissible for the entailed to subsist together with the contradictory of the entailer, so it would be permissible for the entailed to occur without the entailer.' This implies that the entailment is understood as: if A is the entailer and B the entailed, then B cannot be true unless A is true. That is a stronger, biconditional-like relation. This is common in post-Avicennian logic where luzum often implies necessary connection. So follow the text: if B and ¬A cannot coexist, then B requires A. So the proof works.
The exact notion of luzum here includes a condition of necessary connection.
3
Trace implication → man' al-khuluw
Similarly, assume A→B holds. If man' al-khuluw between ¬B and A were false, then ¬B and A could both be false, i.e., B true and A false. Again, B true without A breaks the entailment. So implication forces that ¬B and A cannot both be false (i.e., at least one is true).
This is equivalent to the contrapositive: if B is false, then A is false, i.e., ¬B→¬A.
4
Trace each disjunction → implication
Now prove the reverse: if man' al-jam' holds between two things X and Y, then X implies ¬Y (and Y implies ¬X). Proof: if X were true and Y true, they'd coexist, violating man' al-jam'. So if X is true, Y must be false. Similarly, if man' al-khuluw holds between X and Y, then ¬X implies Y (and ¬Y implies X). Proof: if ¬X were true (X false) and Y false, then both would be false, violating man' al-khuluw. So if X is false, Y must be true.
These correspond to the standard equivalences: (X→¬Y) ≡ ¬(X∧Y) and (¬X→Y) ≡ (X∨Y).
5
Explore true disjunction and non-true disjunctions
The author then shows that the true disjunction (both man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw) entails four conditionals (affirming one denies the other; denying one affirms the other). Then he proves that each non-true disjunction implies the other over the contradictories: man' al-jam' between X and Y gives man' al-khuluw between ¬X and ¬Y, and vice versa. These are analogous to the square of opposition.
This completes the network of entailments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنّ اللزوم بين الأمرين يستلزم الانفصالين فلأنه لولا ذلك لبطل اللزوم بينهما، فإنّه على تقدير اللزوم بين أمرين لولم يصدق منع الجمع بين عين الملزوم ونقيض اللازم لجاز ثبوت الملزوم مع نقيض اللازم فيجوز وقوع الملزوم بدون اللازم، فيبطل الملازمة بينهما - هذا خلف. وكذلك لو لم يصدق منع الخلو بين نقيض الملزوم وعين اللازم لجاز ارتفاع نقيض الملزوم وعين اللازم فيجوز ثبوت الملزوم بدون اللازم، فيبطل اللزوم بينهما - هذا خلف . وأما أن الانفصالين متعاكسان على اللزوم : فلأنه لولاه لبطل الانفصال فإنّه إذا تحقق منع الجمع بين أمرين فلو لم يجب ثبوت نقيض الآخر على تقدير عين كل واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت عين الآخر على ذلك التقدير، فيجوز اجتماع العينين فلايكون بينهما منع الجمع، وكذلك إذا الخلو بين أمرين، فلولم يجب ثبوت عين الآخر على تقدير تحقق نقيض كل واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت نقيض الآخر، على ذلك التقدير فيجوز ارتفاعهما، فلايكون بينهما منع الخلو. والمنفصلة الحقيقية تستلزم أربع متصلات : مقدم متصلتين عين أحد الجزأين، وتاليهما نقيض الآخر، ومقدّم أخريين نقيض أحد الجزأين، وتاليهما عين الآخر ، أي متى صدق الانفصال الحقيقي بين أمرين استلزم عين كل واحد منهما نقيض الآخر، ونقيض كل واحد منهما عين الآخر. وأما الأوّل فلانه لولم يجب ثبوت نقيض الآخر على تقدير عين كلّ واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت عين الآخر على ذلك التقدير فيجوز اجتماعهما، وكان بينهما انفصال حقيقي - هذا خلف. وأما الثاني فلانه لو لم يجب ثبوت عين الآخر على تقدير نقيض كل واحد منهما لجاز ثبوت نقيض الآخر على تقدير نقيض كل واحد منهما، فيجوز ارتفاع الجزأين، فلايكون بينهما انفصال حقيقي والمقدر خلافه - هذا خلف. وكل واحدة من غير الحقيقية - أي من مانعتي الجمع والخلو - تستلزم الأخرى من نقيضي جزأيهما ، فمتى صدق منع الجمع بين أمرين صدق منع الخلو بين نقيضيهما، فإنّه لوجاز ارتفاع النقيضين لجاز اجتماع العينين، فلايكون بينهما منع الجمع، ومهما صدق منع الخلو بين أمرين منع الجمع بين نقيضيهما، فإنّه لوجاز اجتماع النقيضين لجاز صدق ارتفاع العينين، فلايكون بينهما منع الخلو.
And as for the fact that implication between two things entails the two disjunctions: it is because if it were not so, the implication between them would be invalid. For, given implication between two things, if the prevention of combination between the same thing as the entailed and the contradictory of the entailer were not true, then it would be permissible for the entailed to subsist together with the contradictory of the entailer, so it would be permissible for the entailed to occur without the entailer, and thus the entailment between them would be invalid—this is a contradiction. Similarly, if the prevention of void between the contradictory of the entailed and the same thing as the entailer were not true, then it would be permissible for the contradictory of the entailed and the same thing as the entailer to be negated [both], so it would be permissible for the entailed to subsist without the entailer, and thus the entailment between them would be invalid—this is a contradiction. And as for the fact that the two disjunctions are reciprocal [equivalent] to implication: it is because if it were not so, the disjunction would be invalid. For if prevention of combination between two things is realized, then if the subsistence of the contradictory of the other were not necessary given the same thing of each one of them, then it would be permissible for the same thing of the other to subsist on that assumption, so it would be permissible for the two same things to coexist, and thus there would be no prevention of combination between them. And similarly, [if there is] prevention of void between two things, then if the subsistence of the same thing of the other were not necessary given the realization of the contradictory of each one of them, then it would be permissible for the contradictory of the other to subsist on that assumption, so it would be permissible for both to be negated [i.e., to be absent], and thus there would be no prevention of void between them. And the true disjunction entails four conditionals: the antecedents of two conditionals are the same of one of the two parts, and their consequents are the contradictory of the other; and the antecedents of another two are the contradictory of one of the two parts, and their consequents are the same of the other. That is, whenever a true disjunction between two things is true, it entails that the same of each one of them implies the contradictory of the other, and the contradictory of each one of them implies the same of the other. As for the first [entailment], it is because if the subsistence of the contradictory of the other were not necessary given the same of each one of them, then it would be permissible for the same of the other to subsist on that assumption, so it would be permissible for the two [both] to coexist, and there would be a true disjunction between them—this is a contradiction. And as for the second [entailment], it is because if the subsistence of the same of the other were not necessary given the contradictory of each one of them, then it would be permissible for the contradictory of the other to subsist on the assumption of the contradictory of each one of them, so it would be permissible for both parts to be negated [i.e., to be absent], and thus there would be no true disjunction between them, while the opposite was assumed—this is a contradiction. And each one of the non-true [disjunctions]—i.e., the two preventions: of combination and of void—entails the other from the negations of their two parts. So whenever prevention of combination between two things is true, prevention of void between their two contradictories is true. For if the negation of [i.e., rising/absence of] the two contradictories were permissible, then the coexistence of the two same things would be permissible, and thus there would be no prevention of combination between them. And whenever prevention of void between two things is true, [there is] prevention of combination between their two contradictories. For if the coexistence of the two contradictories were permissible, then it would be permissible for the two same things to be negated [i.e., to be absent], and thus there would be no prevention of void between them.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage from a commentary on the Shamsiyya (likely by Qutb al-Din al-Razi) proves a series of interrelations between implication (luzum) and the two types of disjunction: prevention of combination (man' al-jam') and prevention of void (man' al-khuluw). The author first argues that implication between two things entails both prevention of combination and prevention of void. The proof is by contradiction: if prevention of combination between the entailed and the contradictory of the entailer were not true, then the entailed could occur without the entailer, breaking the implication. Similarly for prevention of void. Next, the author shows that both preventions are reciprocal with implication: if prevention of combination holds, then from the affirmation of one thing, the denial of the other must follow, otherwise the two could coexist and prevention would fail. The same reasoning applies for prevention of void. The true disjunction (which combines both preventions) is then shown to entail four conditional propositions: affirming each part implies denying the other, and denying each implies affirming the other. This is proven by similar contradictions. Finally, the author explains that each non-true disjunction (prevention of combination alone or prevention of void alone) entails the other type when the parts are replaced by their contradictories. This logical system mirrors the relationships in Aristotle's square of opposition but applied to conditional statements. The argument is rigorous and typical of post-Avicennian logic.

Key Terms
اللزوم
منع الجمع
منع الخلو
المنفصلة الحقيقية

B) The contradictory of B and the same of A cannot both be absent (man' al-khuluw).

Key Takeaway
Implication and the two types of disjunction (man' al-jam' and man' al-khuluw) are logically interdefinable in a precise way, as proven by contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Definition of Qiyas and Its Centrality in Logic
Definition of qiyas and its primacy in logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qiyas using its genus and differentia, and explain why it is considered the most important tool in logic for acquiring certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Qiyas is the logical tool that turns premises into certain knowledge.

This passage opens the third article on qiyas. It gives the precise definition of a syllogism and then explains why the entire logical enterprise revolves around this concept. Understanding this definition is the key to unlocking the rest of qiyas theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-qiyas' (القياس). What exactly is being defined?
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'qawl mu'allaf min qadaya' – a statement composed of premises. This places qiyas in the broader category of composite statements.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is 'idha sullimat lazima 'anha lidhatiha qawl akhar' – if admitted, necessarily yields another statement by itself. This distinguishes qiyas from other composite statements that do not guarantee a necessary conclusion.
4
Check the definition's completeness
Does this definition include all forms of qiyas (categorical, conditional, disjunctive)? Does it exclude non-syllogistic arguments like induction or analogy? The definition is broad enough to cover the standard types.
5
Understand why qiyas is central
The author argues that assents (tasdiqat) are the ultimate goal of sciences, and qiyas is the means to obtain certain assents. Concepts are only means. Hence logic is primarily about qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في القياس وفيها خمسة فصول الفصل الأول في تعريف القياس وأقسامه القياس قول مؤلّف من قضايا إذا سلمت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر. أقول : المقصد الأقصى و المطلب الأعلى من الفن الكلام في القياس لأنه العمدة في استحصال المطالب التصديقية. وذلك لأن مقاصد العلوم المدوّنة هي مسائلها التي إدرا كانها تصديقات فالمقصود في تلك العلوم هو الإدراكات التصديقية، وأما الإدراكات التصورية فإنما تطلب فيها لكونها وسائل إلى تلك التصديقات، والسر في ذلك أن التصديقات الكاملة هي التي وصلت إلى مرتبة اليقين وهذه يمكن تحصيلها بالأنظار الصحيحة في المبادئ القطعية، فصارت مطلوبة في العلوم الحقيقية، والكامل من التصورات ما وصل إلى كنه الحقيقة وذلك متعسّر بل متعذر؛ فلم تطلب التصورات في العلوم الحقيقية إلا لتكون وسائل إلى التصديقات المطلوبة، و لهذا لم تفرد التصورات بالتدوين وإن أمكن ذلك - بخلاف تدوين التصديقات مجردة عن التصورات فإنه محال. وأيضاً التصديقات إدراكات تامة تقنع النفس بها – دون التصورات – فلذلك صارت مطلوبة في العلوم المدوّنة دون التصوّرات، وإذا كان المقصود الأصلي هو العلم التصديقي، كان البحث في هذا الفن عن الطريق الموصل إليه.
The third article on syllogism, and it contains five chapters. The first chapter: On the definition of syllogism and its divisions. Syllogism is a statement composed of premises which, if admitted, necessarily yields by itself another statement. I say: The ultimate aim and the highest demand of the art of discourse (logic) is syllogism, because it is the mainstay in obtaining assertoric demands. That is because the purposes of the codified sciences are their issues, the perceptions of which are assents; so the intended thing in those sciences is assertoric perceptions. As for conceptual perceptions, they are sought therein only because they are means to those assents. The secret of that is that complete assents are those that have reached the level of certainty, and these can be obtained by correct reflections on certain principles, so they become sought-after in the true sciences. The perfect of concepts is what reaches the inner reality, and that is difficult, nay impossible; so concepts are not sought in the true sciences except to be means to the sought-after assents. For this reason, concepts are not compiled into a separate discipline, even though that is possible—unlike the compilation of assents stripped of concepts, for that is impossible. Also, assents are complete perceptions that satisfy the soul, unlike concepts, so they become sought-after in the codified sciences without concepts. And since the primary objective is assertoric knowledge, the inquiry in this art is about the path that leads to it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage opens the third article on syllogism (qiyas) in the Qutbi sharh. It first gives the standard definition: qiyas is a composite statement (qawl mu'allaf) made up of premises (qadaya) that, if conceded, necessarily entail another statement by themselves (lidhatiha). This definition emphasizes self-contained validity—the entailment is intrinsic, not dependent on external factors. The author then explains why qiyas is the most important topic in logic. He argues that the real aim of all sciences is to obtain assertoric knowledge (tasdiqat), i.e., judgments of truth and certainty, not mere concepts (tasawwurat). Concepts are only sought as means. Complete assents reach the level of certainty (yaqin), which can be achieved through correct reasoning (anzar sahiha) from certain principles (mabadi' qat'iyya). Concepts, on the other hand, cannot fully grasp the essence of things, so they are never ends in themselves. Hence the entire art of logic is focused on qiyas, because it is the instrument (al-'umda) for attaining these sought-after certain assents. The author concludes that since the primary objective is assents, the inquiry in logic must be about the path (tariq) that leads to them, i.e., qiyas.

Key Terms

قول
قضية

According to the passage, why is qiyas the most important topic in logic?
A) Because it is easier to understand than other topics.
B) Because it is the primary tool for obtaining certain assents (tasdiqat).
C) Because it was favored by Aristotle.
D) Because it helps in forming concepts (tasawwurat).

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas as a self-contained argument that yields a necessary conclusion is the foundation of logical inference in the Islamic tradition.

Example of Qiyas and Its Division into Conceptual and Uttered
Author clarifies definition with example and distinguishes conceptual from uttered qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conceptual from uttered syllogism and identify the categorical syllogism form in the example given.

Core Idea
The true syllogism is mental; uttered words merely point to it.

This passage presents the classic example of a categorical syllogism used to define qiyas: 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated.' It then divides qiyas into two kinds: conceptual (ma'qul) and uttered (malfuz). Understanding this division is essential for grasping the nature of logical inference – that validity resides in meaning, not in verbal expression.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of qiyas
Read the definition: 'A statement composed of propositions which, when accepted, entail by themselves another statement.' Note the three key parts: it is a statement, composed of more than one proposition, and it yields a new conclusion necessarily.
The definition excludes single propositions and invalid reasoning.
2
Analyze the example
The example is: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated.' Identify the two premises and the conclusion. Recognize that the middle term 'changing' connects the premises.
This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara).
3
Distinguish conceptual from uttered syllogism
The conceptual syllogism is composed of mental propositions; it is the real syllogism. The uttered syllogism is composed of spoken propositions; it is called syllogism only because it indicates the conceptual one.
The definition can be applied to both, but the conclusion is always conceptual.
4
Understand flexibility of the definition
The commentator explains that the same definition works for both types by interpreting 'statement' and 'propositions' either mentally or verbally. This shows the definition's generality.
Learn how a single definition can cover two distinct but related objects.

Its definition is: 'A statement composed of propositions which, when accepted, entail by themselves another statement' — such as our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' — for it is a statement composed of two propositions; if we accept them, there follows from them by themselves another statement, namely 'The world is originated.' So the statement — that is, the composite — is either a conceptual intellective, which is the genus of the conceptual syllogism, or an uttered, which is the genus of the uttered syllogism. (1) He means that the syllogism is either conceptual — composed of conceptual propositions — or spoken — composed of uttered propositions. The first is the syllogism in reality, and the second is called syllogism only because it indicates the first. This definition can be made a definition for each of them: if made a definition for the conceptual syllogism, by 'statement' and 'propositions' are meant conceptual things; if made a definition for the spoken, by them are meant uttered things; and in both cases, by 'the other statement' — which is the conclusion — is meant the conceptual statement, because uttering the conclusion is not necessary for either the conceptual or the spoken syllogism (Sharif).
