Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of the definition of qiyas with a concrete example: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated.' This is a classic syllogism in the Islamic tradition. The commentator then divides qiyas into two types: conceptual (ma'qul) — the mental composition of propositions — and uttered (manṭuq or masmu') — the spoken expression. The conceptual syllogism is the real syllogism; the uttered is called syllogism only because it indicates the conceptual one. The definition can apply to both, with 'statement' and 'propositions' interpreted accordingly. However, the conclusion (the 'other statement') is always conceptual, regardless of whether the premises are conceptual or uttered, because the syllogism's validity is a mental relation, not dependent on verbal expression.

The Syllogism

Definition of Syllogism and Its Conditions
Author defines syllogism with conditions and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions of a valid syllogism from the definition and distinguish syllogism from other forms of inference such as induction, analogy, and enthymemes.

Core Idea
The syllogism is defined by its composite structure, hypothetical grantability, and intrinsic entailment.

In this lesson, we unpack the classical definition of the syllogism (qiyas) as given by the author. By examining each phrase, you will understand what makes an argument a syllogism and how it differs from induction, analogy, and single propositions. The definition includes conditions that ensure the inference is formally valid and self-contained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum: 'qiyas' (syllogism). The author gives a formal definition immediately. Note that the definition is a composite saying (qawl) that entails another saying.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'composite saying' (qawl mu'allaf). The differentia is 'composed of propositions which, when granted, entail by themselves another saying'. This distinguishes syllogism from other composites like single propositions or definitions.
The definition can be applied to both intellectual and uttered syllogisms by interpreting 'saying' and 'propositions' accordingly.
3
Break down the three conditions
Condition 1: 'composed of propositions' (more than one proposition) excludes single propositions. Condition 2: 'when granted' (mata sullimat) means the premises need not be true; only their hypothetical acceptance. Condition 3: 'entail by themselves' (li dhatiha) means the entailment is intrinsic, without hidden premises.
The example of equality syllogism (A=B, B=C) fails condition 3 because it requires an extraneous premise about transitivity.
4
Test the definition against examples and counterexamples
Consider the example given: 'The world is changing, everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated.' It satisfies all conditions. Contrast with a single proposition that entails its converse, or with induction and analogy, where the conclusion does not necessarily follow.
False premises are allowed as long as the entailment holds—e.g., 'Every human is stone, every stone is inanimate' still qualifies as a syllogism.
5
Understand the division into intellectual and uttered syllogism
The commentary (Sharif) explains that the syllogism can be intellectual (mental) or uttered (linguistic). The definition covers both. The real syllogism is the intellectual one; the uttered is called syllogism only because it signifies the intellectual.
This division shows the scope of logic: it deals with mental reasoning as well as its verbal expression.

Its definition is: 'A composite saying composed of propositions which, when granted, entail by themselves another saying.' Like our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' For it is a composite saying of two propositions: if we grant them, they entail by themselves another saying—which is 'the world is originated.' So the saying—which is the composite—is either a mental concept—which is the genus of the intellectual syllogism—or a uttered expression—which is the genus of the uttered syllogism. And by 'propositions' is meant more than one proposition, to include the simple syllogism composed of two propositions as we mentioned—and the composite syllogism of more than two propositions. It is included in the primary purpose in relation to the investigation of that which leads to conception, because the state of that which leads in this art is like the state of that which is led to in the demonstrative sciences. Then that which leads to assent is divided into syllogism, induction, and analogy, but the most important among them and that which yields certain knowledge is the syllogism. So discussion of it became the ultimate purpose and highest aim in this art in relation to discussion of that which leads to conception, and in relation to all other things that lead to assent. For this reason induction and analogy were made appendages and followers of the syllogism (Sharif). (1) Meaning that the syllogism is either intellectual—composed of intelligible propositions—or audible—composed of uttered propositions. The first is the syllogism in reality, and the second is only called syllogism because it indicates the first. And this definition can be made a definition for each of them. For if it is made a definition of the intellectual syllogism, by 'saying' and 'propositions' are meant intellectual matters; and if it is made a definition of the audible, by them are meant uttered matters. And in both cases, by the 'other saying'—which is the conclusion—is meant the intellectual saying, because uttering the conclusion is not necessary for either the intellectual or the audible syllogism (Sharif). As will come, and he guarded by it against the single proposition that entails by itself its converse or its contrapositive, for it is not called a syllogism. And his statement: 'when they are granted' indicates that those propositions do not have to be granted in themselves, but rather they must be such that if they were granted they would entail another saying; so that the syllogism with true premises and false premises is included in the definition, like our saying: 'Every human is stone, and every stone is inanimate.' For these two propositions, even if false, are such that if they were granted they would entail that 'Every human is inanimate.' And his statement: 'they entail' excludes induction and analogy, for their premises, if granted, do not necessitate anything, because it is possible that what they indicate may not follow from them. And his statement: 'by themselves' guards against what entails not by itself—but rather by means of an extraneous premise—as in the syllogism of equality, which is composed of two propositions the predicate of the first being the subject of the other, like our saying: 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C.' For they entail that 'A is equal to C' but not by themselves, but rather by an extraneous premise, which is that everything equal to something equal to something is equal to it. Therefore that entailment is not realized except when this premise is true, as in our saying: 'A is necessary condition for B, and B is necessary condition for C, so A is necessary condition for C' because what is necessary condition of the necessary condition is a premise.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of the syllogism (qiyas). The author begins by stating the definition: 'A composite saying composed of propositions which, when granted, entail by themselves another saying.' He then provides an example: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated'—these two propositions, when granted, entail the conclusion 'The world is originated.' The definition includes two key aspects: the structure (composite of propositions) and the condition (entailment by themselves when granted). The author then explains that the 'saying' (qawl) in the definition refers to either a mental concept (ma'qul) or an uttered expression (manfuz). This division shows that the syllogism can be considered in two forms: the intellectual syllogism (qiyas ma'qul), which is the real syllogism, and the uttered syllogism, which is called syllogism because it indicates the intellectual one. The definition can be applied to both by interpreting 'saying' and 'propositions' accordingly. The passage then discusses the purpose of studying the syllogism: the path leading to assent (tasdiq) is divided into syllogism, induction (istiqra), and analogy (tamthil). Among these, the syllogism is the most important because it yields certain knowledge. Therefore, the discussion of the syllogism is the ultimate purpose of this art, and induction and analogy are treated as appendages. The definition excludes single propositions that entail their converse or contrapositive by themselves; such propositions are not called syllogisms because they are not composites. The author then explains each phrase of the definition: 'when they are granted' (mata sullimat) ensures that the premises do not have to be actually true; they only need to be such that if granted, they would entail the conclusion. This includes false premises, as in the example 'Every human is stone, and every stone is inanimate.' 'They entail' (lazima 'anha) excludes induction and analogy because their premises do not necessitate the conclusion; the conclusion may not follow. 'By themselves' (li dhatiha) guards against entailment that requires an extraneous premise, as in the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat): 'A is equal to B, and B is equal to C' entails 'A is equal to C' only with the additional premise that 'Everything equal to something equal to something is equal to that thing.' Without that hidden premise, the entailment is not valid. The author gives another example with 'necessary condition' to illustrate the same point.

قول
قضايا

Key Takeaway
The validity of a syllogism depends on its formal structure, not the truth of its premises, as long as the entailment is intrinsic and requires no hidden premises.

The Syllogism

Division of Qiyas into Istithna'i and Iqtirani
Author divides qiyas into conditional and conjunctive with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qiyas istithna'i and qiyas iqtirani by checking whether the conclusion or its contradictory appears among the premises.

Core Idea
The difference is whether the conclusion is stated in the premises.

In this passage, the author defines the two main divisions of syllogism. The key distinction is whether the conclusion itself is explicitly mentioned within the premises. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of logical arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of syllogism
Given a syllogism, first read it carefully. Determine if it is a conditional (if-then) or categorical (all/some) form.
Istithna'i often has a conditional premise; iqtirani is typically categorical.
2
Locate the conclusion
Determine what the syllogism is trying to prove. Look for the final statement that follows from the premises.
The conclusion may not be explicitly stated in some cases.
3
Check if conclusion appears as a premise
See if the exact conclusion or its contradictory (naqid) is mentioned in the premises. If yes, it's istithna'i; if no, it's iqtirani.
For example, in 'If A then B; A, therefore B', B appears as the consequent premise.
4
Apply to examples
Test with the given examples: 'If it is a body, then it is spatial; it is a body' → conclusion 'it is spatial' appears → istithna'i. The incomplete example 'Every body is composed...' likely leads to a conclusion not mentioned → iqtirani.
The iqtirani example is cut off but the definition is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وهو استثنائي إن كان عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل، كقولنا: إن كان هذا جسماً فهو متحيّز، لكنه جسم» ينتج أنه متحيز، وهو بعينه مذكور فيه؛ ولوقلنا : « لكنّه ليس بمتحيّز» ينتج أنه ليس بجسم ونقيضه مذكور فيه. واقتراني إن لم يكن كذلك كقولنا كل جسم مؤلّف وكلّ مؤلف
He said: It is istithna'i if the very conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned in it, as in our statement: 'If this is a body, then it is spatial; but it is a body,' it yields that it is spatial, and it is exactly mentioned in it; and if we say: 'But it is not spatial,' it yields that it is not a body, and its contradictory is mentioned in it. And iqtirani if it is not like that, as in our statement: 'Every body is composed, and every composed...'
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents the division of qiyas into two types: istithna'i (conditional/exceptive) and iqtirani (conjunctive). The author defines istithna'i as a syllogism where either the conclusion itself or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned among the premises. Two examples are given: first, a conditional syllogism with a positive premise: 'If this is a body, then it is spatial; but it is a body' — the conclusion 'it is spatial' is explicitly stated as the consequent. Second, with a negative premise: 'But it is not spatial' yields the contradictory of the antecedent 'it is not a body'. In both cases, the conclusion or its contradictory appears directly. Iqtirani is defined negatively: it is a syllogism where the conclusion is not explicitly mentioned. The example given is an incomplete syllogism: 'Every body is composed, and every composed...' presumably continues with a second premise leading to a conclusion not stated in the premises. This division is foundational for understanding logical forms in the Aristotelian tradition.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'All animals are mortal, and cats are animals, so cats are mortal.'
D) 'Either the prayer is obligatory or supererogatory; it is obligatory, so it is not supererogatory.'

Key Takeaway
The essential division of qiyas is based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated in the premises; this defines istithna'i versus iqtirani.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Valid and Invalid Transitivity in Categorical Entailment
Author illustrates valid and invalid transitive entailment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given relation in a categorical syllogism is transitive and determine if the conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
Not all relations are transitive; the middle term must preserve the relation.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a key principle of categorical reasoning: the validity of a syllogism depends on the transitivity of the relation used in the premises. He gives one valid example and two counterexamples to show when transitivity fails. Understanding this helps you evaluate whether a given argument truly proves its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relation in the premises
Examine the first premise: 'The pearl is in the casket'. The relation is 'in'. The second premise: 'The casket is in the house' also uses 'in'.
The relation is the same across both premises.
2
Check whether the relation is transitive
Determine if the relation holds transitively: if A is in B and B is in C, then A is in C. This works for 'in'.
Transitivity means the property passes from A to C through B.
3
Test the invalid examples
For 'contrary' (mubayin): if A is contrary to B and B is contrary to C, it does not follow that A is contrary to C. For 'half': half of half is a quarter, not a half.
Contrary of contrary is not necessarily contrary; half of half is a quarter.
4
Formulate the general rule
The validity of the conclusion depends on the nature of the relation. Only transitive relations allow the conclusion to follow necessarily.
Always check transitivity before accepting a categorical syllogism.

للشيء ملزوم له. وقولنا : «الدرة في الحقة والحقة في البيت، فالدرة في البيت لأن ما في الشيء الذي هو في شيء آخر يكون فيه.
أما إذا لم تصدق تلك المقدمة لم يحصل منه شيء، كما إذا قلنا : «(ا) مباين لـ(ب) و (ب) مباين ل(ج) » لم يلزم منه أن (ا) مباين ل(ج)، لأن مباين المباين للشيء لا يجب أن يكون مبايناً له.
وكذلك إذا قلنا : «(ا) نصف (ب) و(ب) نصف (ج) » لم يلزم منه أن (ا) نصف (ج)، لأن نصف النصف لا يكون نصفاً.
Entailment of conditionals. For the thing, it is entailed by it. And our statement: 'The pearl is in the casket and the casket is in the house, so the pearl is in the house' because what is in the thing which is in another thing is in it. However, if that premise is not true, nothing follows from it, as when we say: '(a) is contrary to (b) and (b) is contrary to (c)' it does not follow that (a) is contrary to (c), because the contrary of the contrary of a thing is not necessarily contrary to it. And similarly if we say: '(a) is half of (b) and (b) is half of (c)' it does not follow that (a) is half of (c), because half of half is not a half.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains how the validity of a categorical syllogism depends on the transitivity of the relation. The author first gives a valid example: if the pearl is in the casket and the casket is in the house, then the pearl is in the house. This works because the relation 'in' is transitive. Then he presents two counterexamples where transitivity fails. First, if (a) is contrary/mubayin to (b) and (b) is contrary to (c), it does not follow that (a) is contrary to (c); being contrary to a contrary does not guarantee being contrary to the original (e.g., two things can be contrary to the same third thing without being contrary to each other). Second, if (a) is half of (b) and (b) is half of (c), it does not follow that (a) is half of (c); half of half is a quarter, not a half. The lesson is that the middle term must preserve the relation for the conclusion to follow.

Key Terms
تلازم
مباين
نصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the relation is not transitive

C) Because the middle term is not truly predicated
D) Because it is a conditional proposition

Key Takeaway
The validity of a categorical syllogism depends on the transitivity of the relation linking the terms; not all relations are transitive.

Definition of Qiyas and Its Refutation
Definition of qiyas and refutation via immediate inferences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a definition of qiyas that only requires a distinct conclusion is insufficient and distinguish qiyas from immediate inferences like conversion and contraposition.

Core Idea
Not every entailment is a syllogism; a proper qiyas requires a middle term.

In this passage, the author defines qiyas and then critiques that definition by showing it is too broad. The definition requires only that the conclusion be distinct from the premises, but this includes immediate inferences such as conversion (ʿaks) and contraposition (ʿaks naqīḍ). Understanding this critique helps you see the essential component of a syllogism: the middle term that connects the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the phrase 'قول مؤلّف من قضيتين يستلزم لذاته قولاً آخر' (a statement composed of two propositions that by itself entails another statement). This is the definition being critiqued.
The definition is from the previous sentence in the text.
2
Extract the condition
Note that the definition requires the conclusion (قول آخر) to be distinct from each premise. The author says 'يجب أن يكون مغايراً لكلّ واحدة من هذه المقدّمات'.
3
Recognize the counterexamples
The author mentions 'القضية المركبة المستلزمة لعكسها المستوي أو عكس نقيضها' – a compound proposition that entails its own simple converse or contrapositive. For example, 'كل أ هو ب' entails 'بعض ب هو أ' (simple converse) and 'كل غير ب هو غير أ' (contrapositive).
These immediate inferences produce a new statement different from the original proposition, yet they are not considered syllogisms.
4
Understand why they are not qiyas
The reason is that a syllogism requires a middle term (حد أوسط) that links the two premises. Immediate inferences have no such middle term; they simply transform a single proposition. Thus the definition is too broad.
This shows that distinctness of conclusion is necessary but not sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : «قول آخر أراد به أن القول اللازم يجب أن يكون مغايراً لكلّ واحدة من هذه المقدّمات، فإنّه لولم يعتبر ذلك في القياس لزم أن يكون كل قضيّتين قياساً كيف كانتا، لاستلزامها إحداهما. وهذا الحدّ منقوض بالقضية المركبة المستلزمة لعكسها المستوي أو عكس نقيضها ، فإنه يصدق عليها أنها قول مؤلّف من قضيتين يستلزم لذاته قولاً آخر، لكن لايسمى قياساً.
And his statement: 'Another statement' meaning that the necessary statement must be different from each of these premises, because if that were not considered in syllogism, then every two propositions would be a syllogism however they were, because one of them entails the other. And this definition is refuted by the compound proposition that entails its simple converse or the converse of its contradictory, for it is true of it that it is a statement composed of two propositions that by itself entails another statement, yet it is not called a syllogism.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of qiyas (syllogism). The definition requires that the conclusion (the necessary statement) be distinct from the premises; otherwise, any two propositions that entail each other would count as a syllogism. However, this definition is flawed because a compound proposition that entails its own simple converse (e.g., 'All A are B' entails 'Some B are A') or its contrapositive fits the literal definition but is not considered a syllogism. The objection shows that the definition is too broad; a proper definition must involve a middle term, not just any entailment.

Key Takeaway
For an argument to be a qiyas, the conclusion must be distinct from the premises AND there must be a middle term linking them.

The Two Types of Syllogism: Conditional and Categorical
Author defines the two types of syllogism with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given syllogism as istithna'i (conditional) or iqtirani (categorical) by checking whether the conclusion is explicitly stated as a premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism is conditional if the conclusion is stated; otherwise it is categorical.

In Islamic logic, every syllogism falls into one of two categories based on whether the conclusion itself or its contradictory appears verbatim among the premises. This foundational division is the first step in analyzing any argument, and it is essential for correctly applying syllogistic rules in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the proposition that the syllogism aims to prove. This is usually indicated by words like 'therefore' or 'so'.
In the examples, the conclusions are 'it is extended' and 'every body is originated'.
2
Check premises for the conclusion
Look through the premises to see if the exact conclusion or its contradictory (negation) is stated as a whole proposition.
Do not just look for terms; look for the entire proposition.
3
Classify the syllogism
If the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly present, the syllogism is istithna'i (conditional). If neither is present, it is iqtirani (categorical).
The example 'If this is a body, then it is extended; but it is a body' has the conclusion 'it is extended' explicit, so it is conditional. The example 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated' does not have 'every body is originated' as a premise, so it is categorical.
4
Verify with the text's definition
The author defines istithna'i as having the conclusion or its contradictory mentioned 'bil-fi'l' (in actuality). This means the proposition itself is present, not just its terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أقول : القياس إما استثنائي أو اقتراني، لأنه إما أن يكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل أو لا يكون شيء منهما مذكوراً فيه بالفعل.
والأول استثنائي، كقولنا : إن كان هذا جسماً فهو متحيّز، لكنه جسم » ينتج «إنه متحيّز» - فهو بعينه مذكور في القياس - أو «لكنه ليس متحيّز» ينتج : «إنّه ليس بجسم» ونقيضها - أي قولنا : «إنه جسم» مذكور في القياس بالفعل.
وإنما سمي استثنائياً» لاشتماله على حرف الاستثناء، أعني «لكن». والثاني اقتراني، كقولنا : «الجسم مؤلف وكلّ مؤلف محدث، فالجسم محدث فليس هو ولا نقيضه مذكوراً في القياس بالفعل.
وإنما سمي «اقترانياً» لاقتران الحدود فيه.
وإنما قيد ذكر النتيجة ونقيضها في التعريف بالفعل لأنه لو لم يقيد لدخل الاقترانيات في حد القياس الاستثنائي؛ إذ النتيجة مركبة من مادة - وهي طرفاها - ومن صورة - وهي هيأتها التاليفية - ومادتها مذكورة في الاقترانيات، ومادة الشيء مابه يحصل بالقوة، فتكون النتيجة مذكورة فيها بالقوة، فلو أطلق ذكر النتيجة في التعريف لانتقض تعريف الاستثنائي منعاً وتعريف الاقتراني جمعاً.
لا يقال : أحد الأمرين لازم : وهو إما بطلان تعريف القياس أو
91 - The syllogism is conditional and categorical. He said: It is conditional if the very conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it in actuality, as in our statement: 'If this is a body, then it is extended, but it is a body' producing 'it is extended', which is exactly mentioned in it; and if we say: 'but it is not extended' producing 'it is not a body', and its contradictory is mentioned in it. And categorical if it is not like that, as in our statement: 'Every body is composite and every composite is originated' producing 'every body is originated', and neither it nor its contradictory is mentioned in it in actuality. I say: The syllogism is either conditional or categorical, because either the very conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in it in actuality, or none of them is mentioned in it in actuality. The first is conditional, as in our statement: 'If this is a body, then it is extended, but it is a body' producing 'it is extended' - it is exactly mentioned in the syllogism - or 'but it is not extended' producing 'it is not a body', and its contradictory - i.e. our statement: 'it is a body' - is mentioned in the syllogism in actuality. It is called 'conditional' because it contains the particle of exception, namely 'but'. The second is categorical, as in our statement: 'The body is composite and every composite is originated, so the body is originated', and neither it nor its contradictory is mentioned in the syllogism in actuality. It is called 'categorical' because of the conjunction of terms in it. And the condition 'in actuality' was placed in the definition because if it were not restricted, then categorical syllogisms would enter under the definition of conditional syllogism; for the conclusion is composed of matter - which is its two terms - and form - which is its compositional structure - and its matter is mentioned in categorical syllogisms, and the matter of something is that by which it is obtained potentially, so the conclusion is mentioned in them potentially. Therefore, if the mention of the conclusion were unrestricted in the definition, the definition of conditional would be refuted by exclusion and the definition of categorical by inclusion. It is not said: One of the two things is necessary: either the invalidity of the definition of syllogism or...
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage provides the formal division of qiyas (syllogism) into two types: istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (categorical). The author first quotes the matn definition: a syllogism is istithna'i if the very conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated among the premises; otherwise, it is iqtirani. He then elaborates with examples: for conditional, 'If this is a body, then it is extended; but it is a body' yields the conclusion 'it is extended' (explicitly stated). For the other mood, using 'but it is not extended' yields the contradictory of the antecedent. For categorical, 'Every body is composite, every composite is originated' yields 'every body is originated' without the conclusion being stated. The names derive from 'istithna' (exception) due to the particle 'lakin', and 'iqtiran' (conjunction) due to the linking of terms. The key nuance is the phrase 'bil-fi'l' (in actuality): if omitted, categorical syllogisms would wrongly be considered conditional because the matter (terms) of the conclusion are present potentially in the premises. This refinement prevents the definitions from overlapping. The passage ends with an anticipated objection that is cut off.

اقتراني
بِالفعل

D) The conclusion is always affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The classification of syllogisms hinges on whether the conclusion is explicitly present in the premises, which is essential for correct logical analysis.

6 min
Why 'bil-fi'l' Matters in Defining Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains why 'bil-fi'l' is necessary in the definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the phrase 'in actuality' (bil-fi'l) is crucial in the definition of istithna'i and how it prevents categorical syllogisms from being misclassified as conditional.

Core Idea
Without 'bil-fi'l', every categorical syllogism would qualify as conditional because its terms are present potentially.

The passage adds a critical refinement: the conclusion must be mentioned 'bil-fi'l' (in actuality), not just potentially. This prevents the definition from being too broad and ensures that only syllogisms with the explicit conclusion are considered conditional. Understanding this nuance reveals the precision of Islamic logicians.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish matter and form of a proposition
Every proposition has matter (its subject and predicate terms) and form (its quantity, quality, etc.). The conclusion of a syllogism is a proposition with both matter and form.
For example, in 'Every body is originated', the matter is 'body' and 'originated'; the form is universal affirmative.
2
Identify where the conclusion's matter appears
In categorical syllogisms, the terms of the conclusion (its matter) are present in the premises. For instance, in 'Every body is composite; every composite is originated', the terms 'body' and 'originated' appear in the premises.
But the full proposition 'every body is originated' is not stated.
3
Understand 'bil-fi'l' vs 'bil-quwwa'
'Bil-fi'l' (in actuality) means the conclusion is present as a complete proposition. 'Bil-quwwa' (potentially) means only its matter is present, not the form. The conclusion is thus present potentially in categorical syllogisms.
The author says: 'the matter of something is that by which it is obtained potentially, so the conclusion is mentioned in them potentially.'
4
See the consequence of omitting 'bil-fi'l'
If 'bil-fi'l' were removed from the definition of istithna'i, then categorical syllogisms would be included because their conclusion terms are present. This would make the definition too broad and the distinction useless.
The author states that without the restriction, the definition of conditional would be 'refuted by exclusion' (i.e., it would exclude nothing) and the definition of categorical would be 'refuted by inclusion' (i.e., it would include conditionals).
5
Apply to examples
The conditional example has the conclusion 'it is extended' explicitly stated as a premise. The categorical example only has the terms present, not the full conclusion.
Thus, the categorical example is not conditional because the conclusion is not present 'bil-fi'l'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Distinguishing Exceptive from Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author defines the two types of syllogism with conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given syllogism as either exceptive (istithna'i) or conjunctive (iqtirani) by checking whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in the premises.

Core Idea
The type of syllogism is determined by whether the conclusion or its contradictory appears explicitly in the premises.

This lesson introduces the fundamental division of syllogisms into two types: exceptive and conjunctive. The author provides clear criteria and examples to help you distinguish them. Understanding this division is essential for analysing arguments in Islamic logic and related sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Determine the statement that follows necessarily from the premises. This is the conclusion (natija).
In Islamic examples, the conclusion is often the ruling or claim being proven.
2
Check each premise for explicit mention
Look at each premise. Does the conclusion itself appear as a complete proposition? Or does its contradictory appear?
The contradictory (naqid) differs in quality and truth value.
3
Classify based on presence
If the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated in any premise, the syllogism is exceptive. Otherwise, it is conjunctive.
Look for the particle 'lakinna' (but) as a clue in exceptive forms.
4
Verify with examples
Practice with the provided examples: 'If this is a body, then it is spatially located; but it is a body' is exceptive. 'The body is composed; everything composed is originated; so the body is originated' is conjunctive.

أقول : القياس إما استثنائي أو اقتراني، لأنه إما أن يكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل أو لا يكون شيء منهما مذكوراً فيه بالفعل.
والأول استثنائي، كقولنا : إن كان هذا جسماً فهو متحيّز، لكنه جسم » ينتج «إنه متحيّز» - فهو بعينه مذكور في القياس - أو «لكنه ليس متحيّز» ينتج : «إنّه ليس بجسم» ونقيضها - أي قولنا : «إنه جسم» مذكور في القياس بالفعل.
وإنما سمي استثنائياً» لاشتماله على حرف الاستثناء، أعني «لكن». والثاني اقتراني، كقولنا : «الجسم مؤلف وكلّ مؤلف محدث، فالجسم محدث فليس هو ولا نقيضه مذكوراً في القياس بالفعل.
وإنما سمي «اقترانياً» لاقتران الحدود فيه.
وإنما قيد ذكر النتيجة ونقيضها في التعريف بالفعل لأنه لو لم يقيد لدخل الاقترانيات في حد القياس الاستثنائي؛ إذ النتيجة مركبة من مادة - وهي طرفاها - ومن صورة - وهي هيأتها التاليفية - ومادتها مذكورة في الاقترانيات، ومادة الشيء مابه يحصل بالقوة، فتكون النتيجة مذكورة فيها بالقوة، فلو أطلق ذكر النتيجة في التعريف لانتقض تعريف الاستثنائي منعاً وتعريف الاقتراني جمعاً.
لا يقال : أحد الأمرين لازم : وهو إما بطلان تعريف القياس أو بطلان تقسيمه إلى قسمين : لأن الاستثنائي إن لم يكن قياساً بطل التقسيم، وإلا لكان تقسيماً للشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره؛ وإن كان قياساً بطل التعريف، لأنه اعتبر فيه أن يكون القول اللازم مغايراً لكل واحدة من المقدمات ؛ وإذا كانت النتيجة مذكورة في القياس بالفعل لم تكن مغايرة لكل واحدة من مقدماته.
لانا نقول: لا نسلّم أن النتيجة إذا كانت مذكورة بالفعل في القياس لم تكن مغايرة لكلّ واحدة من المقدّمات، وإنما تكون كذلك لو لم تكن النتيجة جزء المقدمة، وهو ممنوع : فإنّ المقدّمة في القياس الاستثنائي ليس قولنا : «الشمس طالعة»، بل استلزامه لوجود النهار.
لا يقال: النتيجة ونقيضها قضية لاحتمالهما الصدق والكذب،
والمذكور في القياس الاستثنائي ليس بقضيّة، فلايكون عين النتيجة
أو نقيضها مذكورين فيه بالفعل.
لانا نقول : المراد بذلك أن يكون طرفا النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورين فيه بالترتيب الذي في النتيجة، وعلى هذا فلا إشكال.
۱) هذا هو التحقيق ، لأن النتيجة لا يمكن أن تكون مذكورة بعينها في القياس - لاعلى أن تكون عين إحدى المقدمتين، ولا أن تكون جزء من إحداهما وإلا لكان العلم بالنتيجة مقدماً على العلم بالقياس بمرتبة أو مرتبتين، وكذلك نقيضها لا يمكن أن يكون بعينه مذكوراً في القياس ، و إلا لكان التصديق بنقيض النتيجة مقدّماً على القياس، ومع التصديق بنقيضها لا يتصوّر
التصديق بها (شريف)
I say: The syllogism (qiyas) is either exceptive (istithna'i) or conjunctive (iqtirani), because either the very conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in it, or neither of them is explicitly mentioned in it.
The first is exceptive, as when we say: 'If this is a body, then it is spatially located; but it is a body' — it produces 'It is spatially located' — so the conclusion itself is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism — or 'but it is not spatially located' — it produces 'It is not a body' — and its contradictory, i.e. our statement 'It is a body', is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism.
It is called 'exceptive' because it includes the particle of exception, i.e. 'but' (lakinna). The second is conjunctive, as when we say: 'The body is composed, and everything composed is temporally originated, so the body is temporally originated' — so neither it nor its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism.
It is called 'conjunctive' because of the conjunction (iqtiran) of the terms in it.
The reason why the explicit mention of the conclusion and its contradictory is qualified by 'explicitly' (bi al-fi'l) in the definition is that if it were not qualified, conjunctive syllogisms would enter the definition of the exceptive syllogism; for the conclusion is composed of matter — i.e. its two terms — and form — i.e. its compositional structure — and its matter is mentioned in conjunctive syllogisms, and the matter of a thing is that by which it exists potentially, so the conclusion is mentioned in them potentially. Thus if the mention of the conclusion were unrestricted in the definition, the definition of the exceptive would be violated by way of exclusion (man'an) and the definition of the conjunctive by way of inclusion (jam'an).
It cannot be said: One of two things is necessary: either the definition of syllogism is invalidated or its division into two parts is invalidated; because if the exceptive is not a syllogism, the division is invalidated; otherwise, it would be a division of a thing into itself and something else; and if it is a syllogism, the definition is invalidated, because it is considered therein that the entailed statement is other than each of the premises; and if the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism, it is not other than each of its premises.
We say in reply: We do not accept that if the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism, it is not other than each of the premises. It would be so only if the conclusion were not a part of the premise, which is denied; for the premise in the exceptive syllogism is not our statement 'The sun is rising', but rather its entailment for the existence of daytime.
It cannot be said: The conclusion and its contradictory are propositions (qadiyya) because they admit truth and falsehood, whereas what is mentioned in the exceptive syllogism is not a proposition, so neither the conclusion nor its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in it.
We say in reply: What is meant by that is that the two terms of the conclusion or its contradictory are mentioned in it in the order that they have in the conclusion. On this there is no difficulty.
(1) This is the verification, because the conclusion cannot be mentioned with its very self in the syllogism — neither as being identical to one of the two premises, nor as being part of one of them — otherwise knowledge of the conclusion would precede knowledge of the syllogism by one or two steps. Likewise, its contradictory cannot be identical to something mentioned in the syllogism, otherwise assent to the contradictory of the conclusion would precede the syllogism, and with assent to its contradictory, assent to it is inconceivable. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the two types of syllogism: exceptive (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). The author begins by stating the criterion: in an exceptive syllogism, the conclusion itself or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned (bi al-fi'l) in the premises, whereas in a conjunctive syllogism, neither is explicitly mentioned. He then provides examples: for the exceptive, 'If this is a body, then it is spatially located; but it is a body' yields the conclusion 'It is spatially located' — the conclusion is explicitly stated. Alternatively, 'but it is not spatially located' yields 'It is not a body' — here the contradictory of the conclusion ('It is a body') is explicitly mentioned. For the conjunctive, 'The body is composed, and everything composed is temporally originated, so the body is temporally originated' — neither the conclusion nor its contradictory appears explicitly. The author notes the naming: 'exceptive' because it includes the particle 'lākinna' (but), and 'conjunctive' because it involves the conjunction of terms. He then justifies the qualification 'explicitly' (bi al-fi'l) in the definition: without it, conjunctive syllogisms would be included under the exceptive definition, because the conclusion's terms (its matter) are mentioned in the conjunctive, even if potentially. This would make the exceptive definition too narrow (man') and the conjunctive definition too wide (jam'). Next, he addresses an objection: if the exceptive syllogism has the conclusion explicit, then it might violate the definition of syllogism (which requires the entailed statement to be different from each premise) or the division into two types. The author replies that the conclusion is not identical to a premise; rather, in an exceptive, the premise is not a simple proposition but an implication (e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'), and the conclusion or its contradictory is part of that implication. Another objection is that the conclusion is a proposition, but what is mentioned is not a proposition. The author clarifies that 'mentioned' refers to the terms of the conclusion arranged in order. Finally, a verification (attributed to Sharif) explains that the conclusion cannot be literally identical to a premise or part of it, as that would involve circularity; rather, it is the terms that are present. This passage ensures a precise understanding of the division.

قياس اقتراني
نتيجة
نقيض

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an exceptive syllogism?
A) Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore, every nabidh is forbidden.
B) If a woman menstruates, she cannot pray; but Fatima is menstruating; therefore, Fatima cannot pray.
C) The world is created; everything created has a creator; therefore, the world has a creator.
D) All angels are obedient; Jibril is an angel; therefore Jibril is obedient.

Key Takeaway
The key to distinguishing exceptive from conjunctive syllogisms is whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly present in the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as exceptive or conjunctive: (1) 'If the sun has risen, then the time for Fajr has passed; but the sun has risen; so the time for Fajr has passed.' (2) 'All prophets are human; Muhammad is a prophet; so Muhammad is human.' (3) 'If a person breaks his fast intentionally, he must expiate; but Zayd broke his fast intentionally; so Zayd must expiate.'

The Syllogism
intermediate

Why 'Explicitly' Matters: Refining the Division
Author refines the division with the qualifier 'bi al-fi'l' and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the qualifier 'bi al-fi'l' (explicitly) is necessary in the definition of exceptive and conjunctive syllogisms, and you will be able to critically evaluate objections against the division.

Core Idea
Without 'bi al-fi'l', every syllogism would be exceptive because the terms of the conclusion are always present potentially.

The author of the passage refines the initial division by adding the qualifier 'bi al-fi'l' (explicitly). This is to prevent conjunctive syllogisms from being mistakenly included under the exceptive definition. The passage also addresses two dialectical objections that test the coherence of the division. This lesson unpacks these refinements.

Guided Path
1
Understand the composition of a conclusion
A conclusion has matter (its terms) and form (its propositional structure). In a conjunctive syllogism, the matter is present but the form is not, so the conclusion is not explicit.
Potential existence (bi al-quwwa) vs actual existence (bi al-fi'l) is a key distinction.
2
See the consequence of omitting 'bi al-fi'l'
If we define exceptive as simply 'conclusion mentioned', then conjunctive would also be considered exceptive because the terms are mentioned. That would collapse the division.
The author says this would violate both definitions: exceptive would be too narrow (man') and conjunctive too wide (jam').
3
Examine the first objection and reply
Objection: If the conclusion is explicit, it is not different from the premises, violating the definition of syllogism. Reply: The premise in an exceptive is a conditional statement; the conclusion is embedded within it, not a separate proposition.
The premise is 'If A then B', not just 'A'.
4
Examine the second objection and reply
Objection: The conclusion is a proposition, but what is mentioned is not a full proposition. Reply: 'Mentioned' means the terms are presented in the order of the conclusion, not the complete proposition.
This clarifies that the presence of terms suffices for the definition.
5
Appreciate the verification from Sharif
The verification shows that the conclusion cannot be literally identical to a premise, as that would lead to circular reasoning. The conclusion's terms can be present without circularity.
This confirms the soundness of the division.

I say: The syllogism (qiyas) is either exceptive (istithna'i) or conjunctive (iqtirani), because either the very conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in it, or neither of them is explicitly mentioned in it.
The first is exceptive, as when we say: 'If this is a body, then it is spatially located; but it is a body' — it produces 'It is spatially located' — so the conclusion itself is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism — or 'but it is not spatially located' — it produces 'It is not a body' — and its contradictory, i.e. our statement 'It is a body', is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism.
It is called 'exceptive' because it includes the particle of exception, i.e. 'but' (lakinna). The second is conjunctive, as when we say: 'The body is composed, and everything composed is temporally originated, so the body is temporally originated' — so neither it nor its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism.
It is called 'conjunctive' because of the conjunction (iqtiran) of the terms in it.
The reason why the explicit mention of the conclusion and its contradictory is qualified by 'explicitly' (bi al-fi'l) in the definition is that if it were not qualified, conjunctive syllogisms would enter the definition of the exceptive syllogism; for the conclusion is composed of matter — i.e. its two terms — and form — i.e. its compositional structure — and its matter is mentioned in conjunctive syllogisms, and the matter of a thing is that by which it exists potentially, so the conclusion is mentioned in them potentially. Thus if the mention of the conclusion were unrestricted in the definition, the definition of the exceptive would be violated by way of exclusion (man'an) and the definition of the conjunctive by way of inclusion (jam'an).
It cannot be said: One of two things is necessary: either the definition of syllogism is invalidated or its division into two parts is invalidated; because if the exceptive is not a syllogism, the division is invalidated; otherwise, it would be a division of a thing into itself and something else; and if it is a syllogism, the definition is invalidated, because it is considered therein that the entailed statement is other than each of the premises; and if the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism, it is not other than each of its premises.
We say in reply: We do not accept that if the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism, it is not other than each of the premises. It would be so only if the conclusion were not a part of the premise, which is denied; for the premise in the exceptive syllogism is not our statement 'The sun is rising', but rather its entailment for the existence of daytime.
It cannot be said: The conclusion and its contradictory are propositions (qadiyya) because they admit truth and falsehood, whereas what is mentioned in the exceptive syllogism is not a proposition, so neither the conclusion nor its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in it.
We say in reply: What is meant by that is that the two terms of the conclusion or its contradictory are mentioned in it in the order that they have in the conclusion. On this there is no difficulty.
(1) This is the verification, because the conclusion cannot be mentioned with its very self in the syllogism — neither as being identical to one of the two premises, nor as being part of one of them — otherwise knowledge of the conclusion would precede knowledge of the syllogism by one or two steps. Likewise, its contradictory cannot be identical to something mentioned in the syllogism, otherwise assent to the contradictory of the conclusion would precede the syllogism, and with assent to its contradictory, assent to it is inconceivable. (Sharif)

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of the two types of syllogism: exceptive (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). The author begins by stating the criterion: in an exceptive syllogism, the conclusion itself or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned (bi al-fi'l) in the premises, whereas in a conjunctive syllogism, neither is explicitly mentioned. He then provides examples: for the exceptive, 'If this is a body, then it is spatially located; but it is a body' yields the conclusion 'It is spatially located' — the conclusion is explicitly stated. Alternatively, 'but it is not spatially located' yields 'It is not a body' — here the contradictory of the conclusion ('It is a body') is explicitly mentioned. For the conjunctive, 'The body is composed, and everything composed is temporally originated, so the body is temporally originated' — neither the conclusion nor its contradictory appears explicitly. The author notes the naming: 'exceptive' because it includes the particle 'lākinna' (but), and 'conjunctive' because it involves the conjunction of terms. He then justifies the qualification 'explicitly' (bi al-fi'l) in the definition: without it, conjunctive syllogisms would be included under the exceptive definition, because the conclusion's terms (its matter) are mentioned in the conjunctive, even if potentially. This would make the exceptive definition too narrow (man') and the conjunctive definition too wide (jam'). Next, he addresses an objection: if the exceptive syllogism has the conclusion explicit, then it might violate the definition of syllogism (which requires the entailed statement to be different from each premise) or the division into two types. The author replies that the conclusion is not identical to a premise; rather, in an exceptive, the premise is not a simple proposition but an implication (e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'), and the conclusion or its contradictory is part of that implication. Another objection is that the conclusion is a proposition, but what is mentioned is not a proposition. The author clarifies that 'mentioned' refers to the terms of the conclusion arranged in order. Finally, a verification (attributed to Sharif) explains that the conclusion cannot be literally identical to a premise or part of it, as that would involve circularity; rather, it is the terms that are present. This passage ensures a precise understanding of the division.

Refuting the Objection Against Conditional Syllogisms
Refutation of an objection against conditional syllogism definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the objection that the conditional syllogism is not a valid syllogism by identifying the role of implication (istilzam) in its premise.

Core Idea
In a conditional syllogism, the premise is the implication, not the simple proposition.

This passage tackles a key objection to the division of syllogisms into conjunctive and conditional types. The objection claims that if the conditional syllogism is a real syllogism, the conclusion would be mentioned in the premise, violating the definition of syllogism. The author refutes this by clarifying that the premise in a conditional syllogism is the implication itself, not a simple proposition. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying conditional logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that if the conditional syllogism is a syllogism, the conclusion would be already present in the premise, making the definition of syllogism (that the conclusion is different from each premise) invalid.
The objection uses the example 'The sun is rising' as a premise.
2
Locate the author's first response
The author responds by denying that the conclusion, if mentioned actually, must be different from each premise. He points out that the premise in a conditional syllogism is not a simple proposition but the implication (istilzam) of the consequent from the antecedent.
Example: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' – the premise is the implication, not 'The sun is rising'.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
A further objection states that the conclusion and its contradictory are propositions, but what is mentioned in the conditional syllogism is not a proposition. The author clarifies that the intended meaning is that the terms of the conclusion (or its contradictory) are mentioned in the order of the conclusion, which is sufficient.
This resolves the issue of the conclusion being 'mentioned' in the premise.
4
Consider the verification (Sharif's note)
The footnote adds that the conclusion cannot be explicitly stated in the syllogism—whether as a whole premise or part of one—lest knowledge of the conclusion precede knowledge of the syllogism. Similarly, its contradictory cannot be stated, as believing the contradictory would preclude believing the conclusion.
This emphasizes the epistemological independence of the conclusion from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان تقسيمه إلى قسمين : لأن الاستثنائي إن لم يكن قياساً بطل التقسيم، وإلا لكان تقسيماً للشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره؛ وإن كان قياساً بطل التعريف، لأنه اعتبر فيه أن يكون القول اللازم مغايراً لكل واحدة من المقدمات ؛ وإذا كانت النتيجة مذكورة في القياس بالفعل لم تكن مغايرة لكل واحدة من مقدماته. لانا نقول: لا نسلّم أن النتيجة إذا كانت مذكورة بالفعل في القياس لم تكن مغايرة لكلّ واحدة من المقدّمات، وإنما تكون كذلك لو لم تكن النتيجة جزء المقدمة، وهو ممنوع : فإنّ المقدّمة في القياس الاستثنائي ليس قولنا : «الشمس طالعة»، بل استلزامه لوجود النهار. لا يقال: النتيجة ونقيضها قضية لاحتمالهما الصدق والكذب، والمذكور في القياس الاستثنائي ليس بقضيّة، فلايكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورين فيه بالفعل. لانا نقول : المراد بذلك أن يكون طرفا النتيجة أو نقيضها مذكورين فيه بالترتيب الذي في النتيجة، وعلى هذا فلا إشكال. ۱) هذا هو التحقيق ، لأن النتيجة لا يمكن أن تكون مذكورة بعينها في القياس - لاعلى أن تكون عين إحدى المقدمتين، ولا أن تكون جزء من إحداهما وإلا لكان العلم بالنتيجة مقدماً على العلم بالقياس بمرتبة أو مرتبتين، وكذلك نقيضها لا يمكن أن يكون بعينه مذكوراً في القياس ، و إلا لكان التصديق بنقيض النتيجة مقدّماً على القياس، ومع التصديق بنقيضها لا يتصوّر التصديق بها (شريف)
The invalidity of its division into two parts: because if the exceptional [syllogism] is not a syllogism, the division is invalid; otherwise it would be a division of the thing into itself and into something else. And if it is a syllogism, the definition is invalid, because it requires in it that the necessary statement be different from each one of the premises; and if the conclusion is mentioned in the syllogism actually, it would not be different from each one of its premises. We say: we do not concede that if the conclusion is mentioned actually in the syllogism it would not be different from each one of the premises; indeed it would be so only if the conclusion were not part of the premise, and this is denied: for the premise in the exceptional syllogism is not our saying 'The sun is rising,' but rather its implication of the existence of daytime. It cannot be said: the conclusion and its contradictory are propositions because they admit truth and falsity, and what is mentioned in the exceptional syllogism is not a proposition, so neither the conclusion itself nor its contradictory are mentioned in it actually. We say: the intended meaning is that the two terms of the conclusion or its contradictory be mentioned in it in the order they have in the conclusion; and on this there is no difficulty. 1) This is the verification: because the conclusion cannot be mentioned itself in the syllogism—neither as being identical to one of the two premises, nor as a part of one of them, for otherwise knowledge of the conclusion would precede knowledge of the syllogism by one or two ranks; and likewise its contradictory cannot be mentioned itself in the syllogism, for otherwise belief in the contradictory of the conclusion would precede the syllogism, and with belief in its contradictory, belief in it is inconceivable. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage critically examines an objection regarding the division of syllogisms into two types (conjunctive and exceptional). The objection claimed that if the exceptional syllogism is not a syllogism, the division is invalid; if it is, then the definition of syllogism (which requires the conclusion to be different from any premise) fails because the conclusion is already mentioned in the premise. The author responds: we do not accept that if the conclusion is explicitly mentioned, it must be different from each premise. The premise of an exceptional syllogism is not a simple proposition like 'The sun is rising' but rather the implication (istilzam) of daytime from that proposition. Thus the conclusion ('Daytime exists') is not the same as the premise. Another objection says that the conclusion and its contradictory are propositions, but what is mentioned in the exceptional syllogism is not a proposition (it is an implication). The author clarifies: the intended meaning is that the terms of the conclusion (or its contradictory) are mentioned in the order of the conclusion, so there is no difficulty. The footnote (by Sharif) affirms: the conclusion cannot be actually stated in the syllogism—whether as a whole premise or part of one—lest knowledge of the conclusion precede knowledge of the syllogism. Similarly, its contradictory cannot be stated, because believing the contradictory would preclude believing the conclusion.

Key Terms

نتيجة
مقدمة
استلزام
نقيض

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms are valid because their premise is the implication, not a simple proposition, ensuring the conclusion is distinct from the premises.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Terms and Premises of a Categorical Syllogism
Author defines terms and premises of categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor term, major term, and middle term in any categorical syllogism, and correctly label the minor and major premises.

Core Idea
The minor and major terms are the subject and predicate of the conclusion; the middle term is the bridge.

Every categorical syllogism has three terms and two premises. The terms are named by their role in the conclusion: the subject is the minor (asghar), the predicate is the major (akbar). The middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (sughra), and the one containing the major term is the major premise (kubra). This foundational vocabulary lets you dissect any syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion (matlub)
Look for the statement that the syllogism aims to prove. It is usually signaled by 'therefore' or 'so'.
In Arabic, the conclusion is often introduced by 'إذاً' or 'فـ'.
2
Identify the minor term and major term
In the conclusion, the subject is the minor term (asghar) and the predicate is the major term (akbar). Write them down.
These are also called the 'extreme terms' (tarafan).
3
Locate the middle term
Scan the two premises for a term that appears in both but is absent from the conclusion. That is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat).
The middle term is the link that connects the extremes.
4
Label the premises
The premise that contains the minor term is the minor premise (sughra). The premise that contains the major term is the major premise (kubra).
If both premises contain the minor term, the minor is the one where the minor term is the subject or predicate as defined in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقال وموضوع المطلوب فيه يسمّى «أصغر» ومحموله: «أكبر». والقضية التي جعلت جزء قياس: «مقدمة». والمقدمة التي فيها الأصغر: «الصغرى» والتي فيها الأكبر: «الكبرى» والمكرر بينهما: «حداً أوسط». واقتران الصغري بالكبري يسمّى «قرينة» و «ضرباً» والهيأة الحاصلة من كيفية وضع الحد الأوسط عند الحدين الأخرى تسمى «شكلاً»، وهو أربعة: لأن الحد الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغري وموضوعاً في الكبرى فهو «الشكل الأول». وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثاني». وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثالث». وان كان موضوعاً في الصغري محمولاً في الكبري فهو «الشكل الرابع». أقول : القياس الاقتراني إما حملي - إن تركب من حمليتين - أو شرطي إن لم يتركب منهما. ولما كان الحملي أبسط فلنبدء به ونقول : القول اللازم باعتبار حصوله من القياس يسمى «نتيجة»، وباعتبار استحصاله منه «مطلوباً». وكل قياس حملي لابد فيه من مقدمتين : إحداهما تشتمل على
He said: The subject of the demand (matlub) is called 'minor' (asghar) and its predicate 'major' (akbar). The proposition made into a part of the syllogism is called 'premise' (muqaddima). The premise containing the minor is called 'minor premise' (sughra) and that containing the major 'major premise' (kubra), and the term repeated between them is called 'middle term' (hadd awsat). The coupling of the minor with the major is called 'qarina' and 'darb' (mood). The form resulting from the manner of positioning the middle term relative to the other two terms is called 'figure' (shakl), and it is four: because if the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major, it is the first figure. If it is predicate in both, it is the second figure. If it is subject in both, it is the third figure. And if it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, it is the fourth figure. I say: The conjunctive syllogism is either categorical if composed of two categorical propositions, or conditional if not composed of them. Since the categorical is simpler, let us begin with it and say: The necessary statement, with respect to its being derived from the syllogism, is called 'result' (natija), and with respect to its being sought from it, 'demand' (matlub). And every categorical syllogism must have two premises: one of them contains...
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage defines the basic components of a categorical syllogism. The author explains that in the conclusion (demand), the subject is the minor term (asghar) and the predicate is the major term (akbar). The propositions used in the syllogism are premises: the one containing the minor term is the minor premise (sughra), the one containing the major term is the major premise (kubra), and the term repeated in both premises is the middle term (hadd awsat). The combination of the minor and major premises is called the qarina or darb (mood). The figure (shakl) is determined by the position of the middle term: (1) predicate in minor, subject in major = first figure; (2) predicate in both = second; (3) subject in both = third; (4) subject in minor, predicate in major = fourth. Then the author proceeds to discuss the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani), which is either categorical (composed of two categorical propositions) or conditional. Since the categorical is simpler, the author begins with it, noting that the derived statement is called the result (natija) with respect to its derivation, and the demand (matlub) with respect to being sought. Every categorical syllogism requires two premises.

أكبر
مقدمة
صغرى
كبرى
حد أوسط

The Four Figures of a Categorical Syllogism
Author enumerates four figures based on middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of a categorical syllogism by analyzing the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure is solely defined by how the middle term is placed in the minor and major premises.

After identifying the terms and premises, the next step is to determine the figure (shakl) of the syllogism. The figure depends entirely on whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise. The source lists four possible arrangements, corresponding to the four figures. Knowing the figure is crucial because only certain figures (especially the first) yield valid conclusions directly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Use the method from the previous lesson to find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
You cannot determine the figure without first isolating the middle term.
2
Check the middle term's position in the minor premise
Look at the minor premise (the one containing the minor term). Is the middle term the subject or the predicate of that proposition?
Minor premise contains the minor term; the middle term could be either the subject or predicate of that premise.
3
Check the middle term's position in the major premise
In the major premise (containing the major term), is the middle term the subject or predicate?
The major premise contains the major term; again, the middle term can be subject or predicate.
4
Match to the figure table
Use the following rule: First figure: middle term is predicate in minor, subject in major. Second figure: middle term is predicate in both. Third figure: middle term is subject in both. Fourth figure: middle term is subject in minor, predicate in major.
Memorize this table; it is essential for evaluating syllogistic validity later.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Components of a Categorical Syllogism
Defines all key syllogism components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor term, major term, middle term, minor premise, major premise, mood, and conclusion in any categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Every categorical syllogism is built from exactly three terms and two premises.

Before you can evaluate the validity of an argument like 'the body is composite, everything composite is originated, therefore the body is originated', you must be able to name its building blocks. This lesson teaches you the precise terminology that logicians use to describe the parts of a categorical syllogism. The passage below is from a commentary on the Shamsiyya, explaining the original text's concise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conclusion
The conclusion (natija or matlub) is what the syllogism tries to prove. In the example given, it is 'the body is originated'. Identify its subject and predicate.
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term (asghar), and its predicate is the major term (akbar).
2
Identify the minor and major premises
The premise that contains the minor term is the minor premise (sughra). The premise that contains the major term is the major premise (kubra). In the example: 'the body is composite' is sughra, 'everything composite is originated' is kubra.
3
Find the middle term
The middle term (hadd awsaf) appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the example, 'composite' is the middle term, linking the minor and major premises.
The middle term is essential for the logical connection.
4
Understand the mood (qarina/darb)
The mood is determined by the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the two premises. For example, 'the body is composite' is particular affirmative, 'everything composite is originated' is universal affirmative.
The exact mood is necessary for checking validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُنَا: وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ فِيهِ يُسَمَّى «أَصْغَرَ» وَمَحْمُولُهُ: «أَكْبَرَ». وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي جُعِلَتْ جُزْءَ قِيَاسٍ: «مُقَدِّمَةً». وَالْمُقَدِّمَةُ الَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَصْغَرُ: «الصُّغْرَى» وَالَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَكْبَرُ: «الْكُبْرَى» وَالْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَهُمَا: «حَدّاً أَوْسَطَ». وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى يُسَمَّى «قَرِينَةً» وَ «ضَرْباً» وَالْهَيْئَةُ الْحَاصِلَةُ مِنْ كَيْفِيَّةِ وَضْعِ الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ عِنْدَ الْحَدَّيْنِ الْآخَرَيْنِ تُسَمَّى «شَكْلاً»، وَهُوَ أَرْبَعَةٌ: لِأَنَّ الْحَدَّ الْأَوْسَطَ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولاً فِي الصُّغْرَى وَمَوْضُوعاً فِي الْكُبْرَى فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الْأَوَّلُ». وَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولاً فِيهِمَا فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الثَّانِي». وَإِنْ كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً فِيهِمَا فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الثَّالِثُ». وَإِنْ كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً فِي الصُّغْرَى مَحْمُولاً فِي الْكُبْرَى فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الرَّابِعُ». أَقُولُ: الْقِيَاسُ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ إِمَّا حَمْلِيٌّ - إِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّتَيْنِ - أَوْ شَرْطِيٌّ إِنْ لَمْ يَتَرَكَّبْ مِنْهُمَا. وَلَمَّا كَانَ الْحَمْلِيُّ أَبْسَطَ فَلْنَبْدَأْ بِهِ وَنَقُولُ: الْقَوْلُ اللَّازِمُ بِاعْتِبَارِ حُصُولِهِ مِنَ الْقِيَاسِ يُسَمَّى «نَتِيجَةً»، وَبِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِحْصَالِهِ مِنْهُ «مَطْلُوباً». وَكُلُّ قِيَاسٍ حَمْلِيٍّ لَابُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ: إِحْدَاهُمَا تَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى مَوْضُوعِ الْمَطْلُوبِ - كَالْجِسْمِ فِي الْمِثَالِ الْمَذْكُورِ - وَثَانِيَتُهُمَا عَلَى مَحْمُولِهِ كَالْحَادِثِ - وَهُمَا يَشْتَرِكَانِ فِي الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ كَـ«الْمُؤَلَّفِ»، فَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ يُسَمَّى «أَصْغَرَ» لِأَنَّهُ يَكُونُ فِي الْأَغْلَبِ أَخَصَّ، وَالْأَخَصُّ أَقَلَّ أَفْرَاداً فَيَكُونُ أَصْغَرَ. وَمَحْمُولُهُ يُسَمَّى «أَكْبَرَ» لِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا كَانَ أَعَمَّ فَهُوَ أَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً. وَالْحَدُّ الْمُشْتَرَكُ الْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَ الْأَصْغَرِ وَالْأَكْبَرِ يُسَمَّى «حَدّاً أَوْسَطَ» لِتَوَسُّطِهِ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيِ الْمَطْلُوبِ. وَالْمُقَدِّمَةُ الَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَصْغَرُ تُسَمَّى «صُغْرَى» لِأَنَّهَا ذَاتُ الْأَصْغَرِ، وَالَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَكْبَرُ «كُبْرَى» لِأَنَّهَا ذَاتُ الْأَكْبَرِ. وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى - فِي إِيجَابِهِمَا وَسَلْبِهِمَا وَكُلِّيَّتِهِمَا وَجُزْئِيَّتِهِمَا - يُسَمَّى «قَرِينَةً» وَ «ضَرْباً». وَالْهَيْئَةُ الْحَاصِلَةُ مِنْ وَضْعِ الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ عِنْدَ الْحَدَّيْنِ الْآخَرَيْنِ - بِحَسَبِ حَمْلِهِ عَلَيْهِمَا أَوْ وَضْعِهِ لَهُمَا، أَوْ حَمْلِهِ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا وَوَضْعِهِ لِلْآخَرِ - تُسَمَّى «شَكْلاً»؛ وَهُوَ أَرْبَعَةٌ. ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ الْأَشْكَالَ الْأَرْبَعَةَ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ.
And our statement: The subject of the sought [conclusion] is called 'asghar', and its predicate 'akbar'. The proposition made part of a syllogism is called 'muqaddima'. The premise containing the asghar is called 'sughra', and the one containing the akbar is called 'kubra', and the repeated term between them is called 'hadd awsaf'. The coupling of the sughra with the kubra is called 'qarina' and 'darb'. The configuration resulting from the manner of placing the middle term with respect to the other two terms is called 'shakl', and it is four: because if the middle term is predicate in the sughra and subject in the kubra, it is the 'first figure'. If it is predicate in both, it is the 'second figure'. If it is subject in both, it is the 'third figure'. If it is subject in the sughra and predicate in the kubra, it is the 'fourth figure'. I say: The conjunctive syllogism is either categorical – if composed of two categoricals – or conditional if not composed of them. Since the categorical is simpler, let us begin with it and say: The statement that necessarily follows, considered in terms of being obtained from the syllogism, is called 'natija', and in terms of being sought from it, 'matlub'. Every categorical syllogism must have two premises: one of them contains the subject of the sought – like 'body' in the mentioned example – and the second contains its predicate – like 'originated' – and they share the middle term – like 'composite'. The subject of the sought is called 'asghar' because it is usually more specific, and the more specific has fewer individuals, so it is smaller. Its predicate is called 'akbar' because since it is more general, it has more individuals. The shared term repeated between the asghar and akbar is called 'hadd awsaf' for its intermediacy between the two extremes of the sought. The premise containing the asghar is called 'sughra' because it has the asghar, and the one containing the akbar is called 'kubra' because it has the akbar. The coupling of the sughra with the kubra – in their affirmation and negation and universality and particularity – is called 'qarina' and 'darb'. The configuration resulting from the placement of the middle term with respect to the other two terms – in terms of being predicated of them or being subject for them, or being predicated of one and subject for the other – is called 'shakl'; and it is four. Then he mentioned the four figures as previously.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section from a commentary (sharh) on the Shamsiyya, explaining the components of the categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli) and its four figures (ashkal). The author first defines the key terms: the subject of the conclusion (matlub) is called 'asghar' (minor term) because it is usually more specific and thus has fewer instances; the predicate of the conclusion is called 'akbar' (major term) because it is more general. The proposition that serves as a premise is called 'muqaddima'. The premise containing the minor term is 'sughra' (minor premise), the one containing the major term is 'kubra' (major premise), and the term repeated in both premises is the 'hadd awsaf' (middle term). The pairing of the two premises together – considering their quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) – is called 'qarina' or 'darb' (mood). The arrangement of the middle term relative to the other two terms gives rise to four figures: (1) first figure: middle term is predicate in the sughra and subject in the kubra; (2) second figure: middle term is predicate in both; (3) third figure: middle term is subject in both; (4) fourth figure: middle term is subject in the sughra and predicate in the kubra. The author then distinguishes conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) into categorical (composed of two categorical propositions) and conditional. Since the categorical is simpler, he begins with it. He further clarifies that the statement necessarily derived from the syllogism is called 'natija' (conclusion) or 'matlub' (what is sought). Every categorical syllogism must have two premises sharing a middle term; the minor term is the subject of the conclusion and the major term is its predicate. The example given uses 'body' (minor term), 'originated' (major term), and 'composite' (middle term), illustrating the classic kalām argument for the creation of the world: The body is composite, everything composite is originated, therefore the body is originated. This passage is an expansion of the original matn, providing the rationale for the naming of each component.

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage (body, composite, originated), which term is the middle term?
A) Body
B) Composite
C) Originated
D) Both body and originated

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism consists of three terms (minor, major, middle) and two premises (minor and major), with the middle term linking the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the minor term, major term, and middle term in the following syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Then name the minor premise and major premise.
?
Given the syllogism: 'The world is created; everything created has a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' Determine the mood (qarina) of this syllogism by stating the quality and quantity of each premise.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Explains the four figures based on middle term position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of any categorical syllogism by identifying the position of the middle term in the minor and major premises.

Core Idea
The figure is determined solely by how the middle term is placed in the two premises.

Once you can identify the middle term, the next step is to note its position in each premise. Is it the subject or predicate in the minor premise? In the major premise? The answer gives you the figure. The passage defines four possibilities, each corresponding to one of the four classical figures. This classification is crucial because figures differ in what conclusions they can validly produce.

Guided Path
1
Write the minor premise first, then the major
Always list the minor premise (sughra) first, then the major premise (kubra), as in the standard ordering. For example: 'Body is composite' (minor), 'Everything composite is originated' (major).
If the premises are given in reverse order, reorder them.
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the example, it is 'composite'.
3
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
For the minor premise, is the middle term the subject or the predicate? For the major premise, is it the subject or predicate? In our example: 'composite' is predicate in the minor (body is composite) and subject in the major (composite is originated).
Use the following key: subject = the term about which something is said; predicate = what is said about the subject.
4
Match the positions to the figure definition
If middle term is predicate in minor and subject in major → first figure. Predicate in both → second figure. Subject in both → third figure. Subject in minor and predicate in major → fourth figure.
Memorize this mapping; it is the heart of the lesson.
