وَقَوْلُنَا: وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ فِيهِ يُسَمَّى «أَصْغَرَ» وَمَحْمُولُهُ: «أَكْبَرَ». وَالْقَضِيَّةُ الَّتِي جُعِلَتْ جُزْءَ قِيَاسٍ: «مُقَدِّمَةً». وَالْمُقَدِّمَةُ الَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَصْغَرُ: «الصُّغْرَى» وَالَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَكْبَرُ: «الْكُبْرَى» وَالْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَهُمَا: «حَدّاً أَوْسَطَ». وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى يُسَمَّى «قَرِينَةً» وَ «ضَرْباً» وَالْهَيْئَةُ الْحَاصِلَةُ مِنْ كَيْفِيَّةِ وَضْعِ الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ عِنْدَ الْحَدَّيْنِ الْآخَرَيْنِ تُسَمَّى «شَكْلاً»، وَهُوَ أَرْبَعَةٌ: لِأَنَّ الْحَدَّ الْأَوْسَطَ إِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولاً فِي الصُّغْرَى وَمَوْضُوعاً فِي الْكُبْرَى فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الْأَوَّلُ». وَإِنْ كَانَ مَحْمُولاً فِيهِمَا فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الثَّانِي». وَإِنْ كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً فِيهِمَا فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الثَّالِثُ». وَإِنْ كَانَ مَوْضُوعاً فِي الصُّغْرَى مَحْمُولاً فِي الْكُبْرَى فَهُوَ «الشَّكْلُ الرَّابِعُ». أَقُولُ: الْقِيَاسُ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ إِمَّا حَمْلِيٌّ - إِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّتَيْنِ - أَوْ شَرْطِيٌّ إِنْ لَمْ يَتَرَكَّبْ مِنْهُمَا. وَلَمَّا كَانَ الْحَمْلِيُّ أَبْسَطَ فَلْنَبْدَأْ بِهِ وَنَقُولُ: الْقَوْلُ اللَّازِمُ بِاعْتِبَارِ حُصُولِهِ مِنَ الْقِيَاسِ يُسَمَّى «نَتِيجَةً»، وَبِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِحْصَالِهِ مِنْهُ «مَطْلُوباً». وَكُلُّ قِيَاسٍ حَمْلِيٍّ لَابُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ: إِحْدَاهُمَا تَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى مَوْضُوعِ الْمَطْلُوبِ - كَالْجِسْمِ فِي الْمِثَالِ الْمَذْكُورِ - وَثَانِيَتُهُمَا عَلَى مَحْمُولِهِ كَالْحَادِثِ - وَهُمَا يَشْتَرِكَانِ فِي الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ كَـ«الْمُؤَلَّفِ»، فَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ يُسَمَّى «أَصْغَرَ» لِأَنَّهُ يَكُونُ فِي الْأَغْلَبِ أَخَصَّ، وَالْأَخَصُّ أَقَلَّ أَفْرَاداً فَيَكُونُ أَصْغَرَ. وَمَحْمُولُهُ يُسَمَّى «أَكْبَرَ» لِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا كَانَ أَعَمَّ فَهُوَ أَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً. وَالْحَدُّ الْمُشْتَرَكُ الْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَ الْأَصْغَرِ وَالْأَكْبَرِ يُسَمَّى «حَدّاً أَوْسَطَ» لِتَوَسُّطِهِ بَيْنَ طَرَفَيِ الْمَطْلُوبِ. وَالْمُقَدِّمَةُ الَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَصْغَرُ تُسَمَّى «صُغْرَى» لِأَنَّهَا ذَاتُ الْأَصْغَرِ، وَالَّتِي فِيهَا الْأَكْبَرُ «كُبْرَى» لِأَنَّهَا ذَاتُ الْأَكْبَرِ. وَاقْتِرَانُ الصُّغْرَى بِالْكُبْرَى - فِي إِيجَابِهِمَا وَسَلْبِهِمَا وَكُلِّيَّتِهِمَا وَجُزْئِيَّتِهِمَا - يُسَمَّى «قَرِينَةً» وَ «ضَرْباً». وَالْهَيْئَةُ الْحَاصِلَةُ مِنْ وَضْعِ الْحَدِّ الْأَوْسَطِ عِنْدَ الْحَدَّيْنِ الْآخَرَيْنِ - بِحَسَبِ حَمْلِهِ عَلَيْهِمَا أَوْ وَضْعِهِ لَهُمَا، أَوْ حَمْلِهِ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا وَوَضْعِهِ لِلْآخَرِ - تُسَمَّى «شَكْلاً»؛ وَهُوَ أَرْبَعَةٌ. ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ الْأَشْكَالَ الْأَرْبَعَةَ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ.
And our statement: The subject of the sought [conclusion] is called 'asghar', and its predicate 'akbar'. The proposition made part of a syllogism is called 'muqaddima'. The premise containing the asghar is called 'sughra', and the one containing the akbar is called 'kubra', and the repeated term between them is called 'hadd awsaf'. The coupling of the sughra with the kubra is called 'qarina' and 'darb'. The configuration resulting from the manner of placing the middle term with respect to the other two terms is called 'shakl', and it is four: because if the middle term is predicate in the sughra and subject in the kubra, it is the 'first figure'. If it is predicate in both, it is the 'second figure'. If it is subject in both, it is the 'third figure'. If it is subject in the sughra and predicate in the kubra, it is the 'fourth figure'. I say: The conjunctive syllogism is either categorical – if composed of two categoricals – or conditional if not composed of them. Since the categorical is simpler, let us begin with it and say: The statement that necessarily follows, considered in terms of being obtained from the syllogism, is called 'natija', and in terms of being sought from it, 'matlub'. Every categorical syllogism must have two premises: one of them contains the subject of the sought – like 'body' in the mentioned example – and the second contains its predicate – like 'originated' – and they share the middle term – like 'composite'. The subject of the sought is called 'asghar' because it is usually more specific, and the more specific has fewer individuals, so it is smaller. Its predicate is called 'akbar' because since it is more general, it has more individuals. The shared term repeated between the asghar and akbar is called 'hadd awsaf' for its intermediacy between the two extremes of the sought. The premise containing the asghar is called 'sughra' because it has the asghar, and the one containing the akbar is called 'kubra' because it has the akbar. The coupling of the sughra with the kubra – in their affirmation and negation and universality and particularity – is called 'qarina' and 'darb'. The configuration resulting from the placement of the middle term with respect to the other two terms – in terms of being predicated of them or being subject for them, or being predicated of one and subject for the other – is called 'shakl'; and it is four. Then he mentioned the four figures as previously.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section from a commentary (sharh) on the Shamsiyya, explaining the components of the categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli) and its four figures (ashkal). The author first defines the key terms: the subject of the conclusion (matlub) is called 'asghar' (minor term) because it is usually more specific and thus has fewer instances; the predicate of the conclusion is called 'akbar' (major term) because it is more general. The proposition that serves as a premise is called 'muqaddima'. The premise containing the minor term is 'sughra' (minor premise), the one containing the major term is 'kubra' (major premise), and the term repeated in both premises is the 'hadd awsaf' (middle term). The pairing of the two premises together – considering their quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) – is called 'qarina' or 'darb' (mood). The arrangement of the middle term relative to the other two terms gives rise to four figures: (1) first figure: middle term is predicate in the sughra and subject in the kubra; (2) second figure: middle term is predicate in both; (3) third figure: middle term is subject in both; (4) fourth figure: middle term is subject in the sughra and predicate in the kubra. The author then distinguishes conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) into categorical (composed of two categorical propositions) and conditional. Since the categorical is simpler, he begins with it. He further clarifies that the statement necessarily derived from the syllogism is called 'natija' (conclusion) or 'matlub' (what is sought). Every categorical syllogism must have two premises sharing a middle term; the minor term is the subject of the conclusion and the major term is its predicate. The example given uses 'body' (minor term), 'originated' (major term), and 'composite' (middle term), illustrating the classic kalām argument for the creation of the world: The body is composite, everything composite is originated, therefore the body is originated. This passage is an expansion of the original matn, providing the rationale for the naming of each component.

The four figures of the categorical syllogism are distinguished solely by whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Components of a Categorical Syllogism
Defines the parts of the categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor term, major term, and middle term in any categorical syllogism, and distinguish the minor and major premises.

Core Idea
In every valid categorical syllogism, the middle term is the bridge that connects the minor and major terms.

This passage defines the basic building blocks of the categorical syllogism: the three terms (minor, major, middle) and the two premises (minor, major). Understanding these components is essential before you can analyse the validity of any syllogism in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (matlub)
Find the statement that is to be proved. It has a subject and a predicate. For example, in 'Every body is temporal originated', 'body' is the subject and 'temporal originated' is the predicate.
2
Label the subject as the minor term (asghar)
The subject of the conclusion is called the minor term because it is usually more specific (fewer individuals) than the predicate.
In the example, 'body' is the minor term.
3
Label the predicate as the major term (akbar)
The predicate of the conclusion is called the major term because it is usually more general (more individuals).
In the example, 'temporal originated' is the major term.
4
Find the middle term (hadd awsat)
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It links the minor and major terms.
In the example, 'composite' is the middle term.
5
Name the premises: sughra and kubra
The premise that contains the minor term is the minor premise (sughra). The premise that contains the major term is the major premise (kubra).
In the example, 'Every body is composite' is the minor premise; 'Every composite is temporal originated' is the major premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوع المطلوب - كالجسم في المثال المذكور - وثانيتهما على محموله كالحادث - وهما يشتركان في الحد الأوسط كـ«المؤلف»، فموضوع المطلوب يسمّى «أصغر» لأنه يكون في الأغلب أخص، والأخص أقل أفراداً فيكون أصغر. ومحموله يسمّى «أكبر» لأنه لما كان أعم فهو أكثر أفراداً. والحد المشترك المكرّر بين الأصغر والأكبر يسمى «حداً أوسط» لتوسطه بين طرفي المطلوب. والمقدمة التي فيها الأصغر تسمّى «صغرى» لأنها ذات الأصغر، والتي فيها الأكبر «كبرى» لأنها ذات الأكبر. واقتران الصغرى بالكبرى - في إيجابهما وسلبهما وكليتهما وجزئيتهما - يسمّى «قرينة» و «ضرباً». والهيأة الحاصلة من وضع الحد الأوسط عند الحدين الآخرين- بحسب حمله عليهما أو وضعه لهما، أو حمله على أحدهما ووضعه للآخر – تسمى «شكلاً»؛ وهو أربعة : لأن الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغرى وموضوعاً في الكبرى فهو «الشكل الأوّل». وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثاني». وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثالث». وإن كان موضوعاً في الصغرى ومحمولاً في الكبرى فهو «الشكل الرابع». وإنما وضعت الأشكال في هذه المراتب لأن الشكل الأول على النظم الطبيعي، فإنّ النظم الطبيعي هو الانتقال من موضوع المطلوب إلى الحد الأوسط ثم منه إلى محموله، حتّى يلزم منه الانتقال من موضوعه إلى محموله، وهذا لايوجد إلا في الأول، فلهذا وضع في المرتبة الأولى. ثم وضع الشكل الثاني لأنه أقرب الأشكال الباقية إليه لمشاركته إياه في صغراه، وهي أشرف المقدّمتين لاشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب الذي هو أشرف من المحمول، إذ المحمول إنما يطلب لأجله إما إيجابا أو سلباً. ثم الشكل الثالث لأن له قربا ما إليه، لمشاركته إياه في أخس المقدمتين. ثم الرابع، إذ لاقرب له أصلاً، لمخالفته إياه في المقدمتين، ويُعده عن الطبع جدا.
The subject of the desired [conclusion] - like 'body' in the mentioned example - and the second of them [the predicate] on its predicate like 'temporal originated', and they share the middle term like 'composite'. So the subject of the desired [conclusion] is called the 'minor term' because it is usually more specific, and the more specific has fewer individuals, so it is smaller. Its predicate is called the 'major term' because since it is more general, it has more individuals. The common term repeated between the minor and major is called 'middle term' due to its intermediacy between the two extremes of the desired [conclusion]. The premise containing the minor term is called 'minor premise' because it is possessed of the minor, and the one containing the major term is called 'major premise' because it is possessed of the major. The conjunction of the minor and major premises - in their affirmation and negation, universality and particularity - is called 'mood'. And the configuration arising from the position of the middle term relative to the other two terms - according to its being predicated upon them or its being subject for them, or its being predicated upon one and subject for the other - is called 'figure'; and it is four: because if the middle is a predicate in the minor premise and a subject in the major, it is the 'first figure'. If it is a predicate in both, it is the 'second figure'. If it is a subject in both, it is the 'third figure'. And if it is a subject in the minor and a predicate in the major, it is the 'fourth figure'. And the figures were placed in this order because the first figure is in the natural arrangement, for the natural arrangement is the transition from the subject of the desired [conclusion] to the middle term then from it to its predicate, such that the transition from its subject to its predicate follows; and this only exists in the first, so for this reason it was placed in the first rank. Then the second figure was placed because it is the closest of the remaining figures to it due to its sharing with it in its minor premise, which is the more honorable of the two premises because it contains the subject of the desired [conclusion] which is more honorable than the predicate, since the predicate is only sought for its sake either affirmatively or negatively. Then the third figure because it has some closeness to it, due to its sharing with it in the baser of the two premises. Then the fourth, because it has no closeness at all, due to its differing from it in both premises, and it is very far from the natural [arrangement].
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion (sharh) on the parts and figures of the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). The author begins by defining the key components of a syllogism using an example: the conclusion's subject (e.g., 'body') is called the minor term (asghar) because it is usually more specific and therefore has fewer individuals; its predicate (e.g., 'temporal originated') is called the major term (akbar) because it is more general and has more individuals. The term they share (e.g., 'composite') is called the middle term (hadd awsat) for its intermediacy. The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise (sughra), and that containing the major is the major premise (kubra). The combination of quantity and quality (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) in the two premises constitutes the 'qarina' or 'darb' (mood). The arrangement of the middle term's position relative to the subject and predicate of the conclusion yields the 'shakl' (figure). Then the author enumerates the four figures: first figure – middle term is predicate in sughra and subject in kubra; second – middle is predicate in both; third – middle is subject in both; fourth – middle is subject in sughra and predicate in kubra. Finally, he justifies the ordering: the first figure is 'natural' because it moves from the minor term to the middle to the major logically; the second shares the minor premise with the first (which is superior), the third shares the major premise (which is inferior), and the fourth has no commonality with the first and is thus farthest from nature. This passage is definitional and descriptive, not argumentative; it sets out the standard classification of syllogistic figures in Aristotelian logic as adapted in the Islamic tradition. For example, in the first figure: 'Every body is composite, and every composite is temporal originated' yields 'Every body is temporal originated'.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Four Figures and Their Natural Order
Lists the four figures with their ordering justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a categorical syllogism into one of the four figures based on the position of the middle term, and explain the rationale for the order of the figures.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by where the middle term sits as subject or predicate in the two premises.

After defining the terms, the author turns to the 'figure' (shakl) of the syllogism, which depends on the arrangement of the middle term. There are exactly four possibilities, and the author explains why they are ordered as they are, from the most natural to the least.

Guided Path
1
Determine the position of the middle term in the minor premise
Look at the minor premise (sughra): is the middle term a subject or a predicate?
For example, in 'Every body is composite', 'composite' is the predicate.
2
Determine its position in the major premise
Look at the major premise (kubra): is the middle term a subject or a predicate?
In 'Every composite is temporal originated', 'composite' is the subject.
3
Apply the definition of each figure
Use the four cases: middle as (predicate in sughra, subject in kubra) = first figure; (predicate in both) = second; (subject in both) = third; (subject in sughra, predicate in kubra) = fourth.
4
Check the natural order justification
The first figure is natural because it moves from subject to middle to predicate. The second shares the minor premise (more honorable), the third shares the major premise (less honorable), and the fourth shares neither.
5
Practice with a new example
Take a syllogism like 'All humans are animals, all animals are living, therefore all humans are living'. Determine its figure.
Here middle term 'animals' is predicate in minor ('humans are animals') and subject in major ('animals are living'), so it is first figure.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Four Figures of Categorical Syllogism
Definition of the four figures by middle term position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of a categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term in the premises.

Core Idea
The position of the middle term determines the figure of a categorical syllogism.

The author defines the four figures by the location of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in the minor and major premises. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing syllogistic arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion, and the major premise contains the predicate. This is the first step in any syllogistic analysis.
2
Locate the middle term
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the hadd al-awsat.
3
Check its position in the minor premise
Determine whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate in the minor premise.
4
Check its position in the major premise
Determine whether the middle term is the subject or the predicate in the major premise.
5
Determine the figure
Use the author's rules: first if predicate in minor and subject in major; second if predicate in both; third if subject in both; fourth if subject in minor and predicate in major.
Memorize these four patterns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغرى وموضوعاً في الكبري فهو «الشكل الأوّل ». وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثاني». وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثالث ». وإن كان موضوعاً في الصغرى ومحمولاً في الكبرى فهو «الشكل الرابع». وإنما وضعت الأشكال في هذه المراتب لأن الشكل الأول على النظم الطبيعي، فإنّ النظم الطبيعي هو الانتقال من موضوع المطلوب إلى الحد الأوسط ثم منه إلى محموله، حتّى يلزم منه الانتقال من موضوعه إلى محموله، وهذا لايوجد إلا في الأول، فلهذا وضع في المرتبة الأولى. ثم وضع الشكل الثاني لأنه أقرب الأشكال الباقية إليه لمشاركته إياه في صغراه، وهي أشرف المقدّمتين لاشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب الذي هو أشرف من المحمول، إذ المحمول إنما يطلب لأجله إما إيجابا أو سلباً. ثم الشكل الثالث لأن له قربا مـا إليه، لمشاركته إياه في أخس المقدمتين. ثم الرابع، إذ لاقرب له أصلاً، لمخالفته إياه في المقدمتين، ويُعده عن الطبع جدا.
Because the middle term, if it is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major, then it is 'first figure'. And if it is predicate in both, then it is 'second figure'. And if it is subject in both, then it is 'third figure'. And if it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, then it is 'fourth figure'. And the figures are arranged in these ranks because the first figure is according to natural order; for the natural order is the transition from the subject of the conclusion to the middle term, then from it to its predicate, so that the transition from its subject to its predicate follows necessarily. And this exists only in the first figure, so it is placed in the first rank. Then the second figure is placed because it is the nearest of the remaining figures to it due to its sharing with it in its minor premise, which is the nobler of the two premises because it contains the subject of the conclusion, which is nobler than the predicate, since the predicate is sought for its sake, either affirmatively or negatively. Then the third figure because it has some nearness to it, due to its sharing with it in the lesser of the two premises. Then the fourth, because it has no nearness at all, since it differs from it in both premises, and it is very far from the natural.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of categorical syllogism and explains why they are ordered as they are. The definition of each figure is based on the position of the middle term in the two premises. In the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major. In the second, it is predicate in both. In the third, it is subject in both. In the fourth, it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The author then provides reasoning for this ordering. The first figure is considered the most natural because it moves from the subject of the conclusion to the middle term and then to the predicate, mirroring the logical transition from subject to predicate. The second figure is placed next because it shares the minor premise with the first figure, and the minor is more noble as it contains the subject. The third figure shares the less noble premise (the major) with the first. The fourth figure shares no premise with the first, so it is farthest. This classification is foundational for understanding syllogistic logic.

الحد الأوسط

A) subject in both premises

Key Takeaway
The four figures are distinguished solely by the position of the middle term in the two premises.

The Natural Order of the Four Figures
Rationale for ordering the four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first figure is considered the most natural and why the others are ordered as they are.

Core Idea
The first figure mirrors the natural order of reasoning from subject to predicate.

The author not only defines the four figures but also provides a rationale for their ordering. The first figure is placed first because it follows the natural progression from subject to middle term to predicate. The others are ranked by their similarity to the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the natural order
The natural order (al-nazm al-tabi'i) is the progression from the subject of the conclusion to the middle term, then to the predicate. This mirrors how the mind naturally moves from a subject to a predicate through an intermediate.
2
Check the first figure
In the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor (connecting to the subject) and subject in the major (leading to the predicate). This follows the natural chain exactly.
The first figure is called 'natural' because it matches this chain.
3
Compare the second figure
The second figure shares the minor premise with the first figure. The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion, which is nobler than the predicate. Hence it is placed second.
The minor premise is more important because it contains the subject.
4
Compare the third figure
The third figure shares the major premise with the first figure. The major premise contains the predicate, which is less noble. So it is placed third.
The predicate is sought for the sake of the subject, so it is less noble.
5
Compare the fourth figure
The fourth figure shares no premise with the first figure; it differs in both. Thus it has no nearness and is placed last, being farthest from the natural order.
Some later logicians reject the fourth figure entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن الأوسط إن كان محمولاً في الصغرى وموضوعاً في الكبري فهو «الشكل الأوّل ». وإن كان محمولاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثاني». وإن كان موضوعاً فيهما فهو «الشكل الثالث ». وإن كان موضوعاً في الصغرى ومحمولاً في الكبرى فهو «الشكل الرابع». وإنما وضعت الأشكال في هذه المراتب لأن الشكل الأول على النظم الطبيعي، فإنّ النظم الطبيعي هو الانتقال من موضوع المطلوب إلى الحد الأوسط ثم منه إلى محموله، حتّى يلزم منه الانتقال من موضوعه إلى محموله، وهذا لايوجد إلا في الأول، فلهذا وضع في المرتبة الأولى. ثم وضع الشكل الثاني لأنه أقرب الأشكال الباقية إليه لمشاركته إياه في صغراه، وهي أشرف المقدّمتين لاشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب الذي هو أشرف من المحمول، إذ المحمول إنما يطلب لأجله إما إيجابا أو سلباً. ثم الشكل الثالث لأن له قربا مـا إليه، لمشاركته إياه في أخس المقدمتين. ثم الرابع، إذ لاقرب له أصلاً، لمخالفته إياه في المقدمتين، ويُعده عن الطبع جدا.
Because the middle term, if it is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major, then it is 'first figure'. And if it is predicate in both, then it is 'second figure'. And if it is subject in both, then it is 'third figure'. And if it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major, then it is 'fourth figure'. And the figures are arranged in these ranks because the first figure is according to natural order; for the natural order is the transition from the subject of the conclusion to the middle term, then from it to its predicate, so that the transition from its subject to its predicate follows necessarily. And this exists only in the first figure, so it is placed in the first rank. Then the second figure is placed because it is the nearest of the remaining figures to it due to its sharing with it in its minor premise, which is the nobler of the two premises because it contains the subject of the conclusion, which is nobler than the predicate, since the predicate is sought for its sake, either affirmatively or negatively. Then the third figure because it has some nearness to it, due to its sharing with it in the lesser of the two premises. Then the fourth, because it has no nearness at all, since it differs from it in both premises, and it is very far from the natural.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of categorical syllogism and explains why they are ordered as they are. The definition of each figure is based on the position of the middle term in the two premises. In the first figure, the middle term is predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major. In the second, it is predicate in both. In the third, it is subject in both. In the fourth, it is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The author then provides reasoning for this ordering. The first figure is considered the most natural because it moves from the subject of the conclusion to the middle term and then to the predicate, mirroring the logical transition from subject to predicate. The second figure is placed next because it shares the minor premise with the first figure, and the minor is more noble as it contains the subject. The third figure shares the less noble premise (the major) with the first. The fourth figure shares no premise with the first, so it is farthest. This classification is foundational for understanding syllogistic logic.

The first figure is the most natural; the others are ranked by how closely they resemble it in structure.

First Figure: Conditions and Four Valid Moods
Author states the conditions for validity of first figure and lists its four moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a given first-figure categorical syllogism satisfies the conditions for validity and identify which of the four moods it exemplifies.

Core Idea
The first figure yields self-evident conclusions only when the minor is affirmative and the major is universal.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational conditions that make the first figure of the categorical syllogism productive. Without these conditions, even a seemingly correct syllogism can fail. The author succinctly states the two conditions (affirmative minor, universal major) and then presents the four moods that satisfy them. This is essential knowledge for anyone who wants to construct or evaluate logical proofs in the Islamic sciences, where the first figure is the most frequently used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Locate the two conditions stated: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. Understand the rationale: a negative minor prevents the minor term from being included under the middle; a particular major risks ambiguity about which part of the major is predicated.
The condition for the minor is about quality, for the major about quantity.
2
Recognize the four moods
List the four moods as described: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major → universal affirmative; (2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major → universal negative; (3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major → particular affirmative; (4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative.
In traditional Western logic, these are Barbara, Celarent, Darii, and Ferio respectively.
3
Map the schematic variables
Note that the author uses ج (C) for the minor term, ب (B) for the middle term, and ا (A) for the major term. Replace these with concrete Islamic examples to see the argument form clearly.
For instance, in a fiqh example: ج = nabidh, ب = intoxicant, ا = forbidden.
4
Test the self-evidence claim
The author says the conclusions of this figure are self-evident (bayyin bi dhatiha). Try to prove one of the moods by using the dictum de omni et nullo or by reduction to impossibility, and confirm that the validity is immediately clear.
This self-evidence is why the first figure is the standard of perfection in categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: أما الشكل الأوّل فشرط إنتاجه إيجاب الصغرى - وإلا لم يندرج الأصغر في الأوسط - وكلية الكبرى - وإلا لاحتمل أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه بالأكبر غير البعض المحكوم به على الأصغر. وضروبه الناتجة أربع الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة كلية، كقولنا : «كل (ج) ب) وكل (با) فكل (ج )». الثاني من كليتين: الصغرى موجبة والكبرى سالبة ينتج سالبة كلية كقولنا: كلّ (ج) (ب) ولاشيء من (ب ا) فلاشيء من (ج ۱)». الثالث من موجبتين، والصغرى جزئية ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا: بعض (ج (ب) وكل (با) فبعض (ج 1)». الرابع من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : «بعض (ج (ب) ولاشيء من (ب) (۱) فبعض (ج) ليس (ا)» ونتائج هذا الشكل بينة بذاتها. القول: اعلم أن لإنتاج الأشكال الأربعة شرائط بحسب كيفية المقدّمات وكميتها وشرائط بحسب جهة المقدّمات. أما الشرائط التي بحسب الجهة فسيأتيك بيانها في فصل المختلطات (۱) وإنما أفرد للشرائط بحسب الجهة فصلاً على حدة ليكون أسهل في الضبط لمباحثه المتكثرة الشعب (شريف).
He said: As for the first figure, the condition for its productivity is the affirmativeness of the minor premise — otherwise the minor term would not be included under the middle — and the universality of the major — otherwise it would be possible that some of what is judged by the major is not the same as some of what is judged on the minor. Its productive moods are four: the first from two universal affirmatives produces a universal affirmative, as in our saying: 'Every C is B, and every B is A, so every C is A.' The second from two universal premises: the minor affirmative and the major negative produces a universal negative, as in our saying: 'Every C is B, and no B is A, so no C is A.' The third from two affirmatives, with the minor particular, produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Some C is B, and every B is A, so some C is A.' The fourth from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Some C is B, and no B is A, so some C is not A.' And the conclusions of this figure are self-evident. He said: Know that for the productivity of the four figures there are conditions regarding the quality and quantity of the premises, and conditions regarding the modality of the premises. As for the conditions regarding modality, their explanation will come to you in the chapter on mixed (moods). And he singled out a separate chapter for conditions regarding modality to make it easier to grasp its numerous branches (Sharif).
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the first figure to produce a valid conclusion and lists its four valid moods. The first condition is that the minor premise must be affirmative; otherwise, the minor term (subject of the conclusion) would not be included under the middle term. The second condition is that the major premise must be universal; otherwise, part of what the major term is predicated of might not be the same part that is predicated of the minor. The four moods are then enumerated using schematic examples with letters (C, B, A). The first mood (Barbara): two universal affirmatives yield a universal affirmative. The second (Celarent): universal affirmative minor and universal negative major yield a universal negative. The third (Darii): particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major yield a particular affirmative. The fourth (Ferio): particular affirmative minor and universal negative major yield a particular negative. The author notes that these conclusions are self-evident (i.e., their validity is clear without need for reduction). Finally, a note is added that conditions regarding modality (like necessity, possibility) will be treated in a later chapter on mixed syllogisms, and a separate chapter is allocated for that due to the abundance of its topics. This passage is part of the Sharh tradition, expanding on the matn's brief mention of the first figure.

First Figure: Conditions and Four Productive Moods
Conditions and four valid moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions of the first figure and list its four productive moods with their premise patterns and conclusions.

Core Idea
The first figure yields conclusions directly and has exactly four valid moods.

The first figure is the most powerful and intuitive syllogistic figure because its conclusions follow self-evidently. The author lays out two strict conditions and four moods that satisfy them. Understanding these conditions is key to testing the validity of any categorical syllogism in this figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find what the author says about the minor and major premises. The minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal.
2
Apply condition 1 (affirmative minor)
Why must the minor be affirmative? The author explains: otherwise the minor term would not be included in the middle term, breaking the connection.
This ensures the minor term is part of the set that the middle term covers.
3
Apply condition 2 (universal major)
Why must the major be universal? Otherwise the part of the middle that the major predicates of might not include the minor term.
A particular major would leave open the possibility that the minor term falls outside the predicated portion.
4
Enumerate the four moods
List the four valid premise combinations: (1) both universal affirmatives, (2) affirmative minor & negative major both universal, (3) particular affirmative minor & universal affirmative major, (4) particular affirmative minor & universal negative major. State each conclusion.
These correspond to Barbara, Celarent, Darii, and Ferio in the Latin tradition.
5
Verify self-evidence
For each mood, test with an Islamic example to see that the conclusion follows necessarily without conversion.
For instance, using the khamr-nabidh example in Barbara.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He said: As for the first figure, the condition for its productivity is that the minor premise be affirmative — otherwise the minor term would not be included in the middle term — and that the major premise be universal — otherwise it is possible that the part predicated of by the major is not the part predicated of by the minor. Its productive moods are four: First, from two universal affirmatives it yields a universal affirmative, as when we say: 'Every J is B and every B is A, so every J is A.' Second, from two universal [premises]: the minor affirmative and the major negative, it yields a universal negative, as when we say: 'Every J is B and no B is A, so no J is A.' And the conclusions of this figure are self-evident.

Commentary
This passage defines the first figure of the categorical syllogism and its conditions for productivity. The author states two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, otherwise the minor term would not be included under the middle term, and (2) the major premise must be universal, otherwise the part of the middle term that is predicated by the major might not include the minor term. Given these conditions, there are exactly four valid moods. The first mood (Barbara) has two universal affirmatives and yields a universal affirmative conclusion. The second mood (Celarent) has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a universal negative. The third mood (Darii) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative. The fourth mood (Ferio) has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a particular negative. The author notes that the conclusions of this figure are self-evident, meaning they follow directly without need for conversion.

Conditions for the First Figure: Minor Affirmative, Major Universal
Author explains conditions for first figure and basis of mood count.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the minor must be affirmative and the major universal in a first-figure categorical syllogism, and identify the four productive moods.

Core Idea
Two conditions govern first figure productivity: minor affirmative, major universal.

In this passage, the author explains that for a first-figure categorical syllogism to yield a necessary conclusion, two conditions must hold: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. He provides detailed logical justifications and shows how these conditions reduce the possible premise pairs to just four productive moods. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Locate and state the two conditions explicitly mentioned: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative in quality, and (2) the major premise must be universal in quantity.
These are necessary conditions for any productive first-figure syllogism.
2
Trace the reasoning for condition 1
The author argues: if the minor were negative, the minor term would not fall under the middle term; consequently, the middle would not connect the minor to the major, and no conclusion follows. Thus, the minor must be affirmative.
This reasoning uses modus tollens: if P (minor negative) then Q (no conclusion); but we want a conclusion (not Q), so not P.
3
Trace the reasoning for condition 2
The author argues: if the major were particular, only part of the middle term has the major term; the minor might not be that part, so the conclusion does not follow. Example: 'Every human is an animal, some animal is a horse' does not yield 'Some human is a horse.'
Ensures the major covers all instances of the middle.
4
Understand proposition types
Propositions can be personal (e.g., 'This is Zayd'), quantified (universal/particular), or neglected (no quantifier). Personal propositions are treated as universal because they produce a conclusion when used as a major in this figure; neglected propositions act as particular.
Only quantified propositions are considered in mood counting.
5
Count the productive moods
With these two conditions, out of 16 possible premise pairs (four types for each premise), only four are productive: AAA (Barbara), EAE (Celarent), AII (Darii), EIO (Ferio). Each has a minor affirmative (A or I) and a major universal (A or E).
The author does not list the moods explicitly but states 'four moods' based on the conditions.

The statement: Know that for the productivity of the four figures there are conditions according to the quality and quantity of the premises, and conditions according to the modality of the premises. As for the conditions according to modality, their explanation will come to you in the chapter on mixed [propositions]... (1) And he only devoted a separate chapter for the conditions regarding modality to make it easier to grasp its many-branched topics (Sharif). As for the conditions according to quality and quantity, in the first figure there are two things: one regarding quality, the affirmation of the minor; the second regarding quantity, the universality of the major. As for the first: because if the minor were negative, the minor term would not fall under the middle term, so productivity would not occur; for the major indicates that whatever has the middle term is predicated of the major, and the minor, if it were negative, would judge that the middle is denied of the minor, so the minor is not included in what has the middle, so the judgment on what has the middle does not extend to the minor, so the conclusion does not follow. As for the second: because if the major were particular, its meaning would be that some of the middle is predicated of the major, and it is possible that the minor is not that part, so the judgment on some of the middle does not extend to the minor, so the conclusion does not follow; for example, it is true that 'Every human is an animal and some animal is a horse' but it is not true that 'Some human is a horse.' And its productive moods considering these two conditions are four, because the possible premise pairs in every figure are sixteen, for you have known that the proposition is restricted to the personal, the quantified, and the neglected, but the personal is treated as universal because it produces in the major of this figure - for if we say: 'This is Zayd and Zayd is a human' it necessarily produces 'This is a human' - and the neglected has the force of particular. So the considered proposition is only the quantified, and it is four: the two universal and...

This passage expands on the conditions for the first figure with detailed reasoning. The author first distinguishes two types of conditions: those related to modality (جهة) and those related to quality and quantity (كيفية وكمية). The modality conditions are deferred to a later chapter. For the first figure, the two quality/quantity conditions are: (1) the minor must be affirmative, and (2) the major must be universal. The justification for the first condition: if the minor were negative, the minor term would not be included under the middle term, so the middle term would not connect the minor to the major. The justification for the second condition: if the major were particular, only part of the middle term has the major term, and the minor might not be that part. The author provides a concrete example: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse' does not yield 'Some human is a horse.' From these two conditions, only four premise pairs are productive out of a possible sixteen. The author notes that propositions can be personal (شخصية), quantified (محصورة), or neglected (مهملة). Personal propositions (e.g., 'This is Zayd') are treated as universal because they produce a conclusion when used as a major in this figure. Neglected propositions (e.g., 'Human is animal') have the force of particular. Thus, only quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) are considered, yielding four possibilities for each premise, but only four combinations satisfy the conditions.

المهملة

Key Takeaway
In the first figure, a valid syllogism requires the minor to be affirmative and the major to be universal.

Four Valid Moods of the First Figure
Author enumerates the four valid moods of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid moods of the first figure and determine the conclusion type from given premises.

Core Idea
The first figure alone produces all four categorical conclusion types.

This passage introduces the four valid moods (ḍarb) of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic, particularly in theological and legal reasoning where the first figure is most used for certain proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand premise types
Recognize the four possible premises based on quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative): universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
These correspond to the four categorical propositions in Arabic: موجبة كلية, سالبة كلية, موجبة جزئية, سالبة جزئية.
2
Apply conditions for first figure
The first figure has two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative, and the major premise must be universal. These conditions eliminate invalid combinations.
As stated in the passage: 'اشتراط الأمر الأوّل أسقط ثمانية أضرب... والثاني أربعة أخرى'.
3
Enumerate the four valid moods
List the four remaining mood combinations: (1) A+A (Barbara), (2) A+E (Celarent), (3) I+A (Darii), (4) I+E (Ferio).
Each mood uses Arabic logical variables: ج (minor), ب (middle), أ (major).
4
Derive conclusions
For each mood, the conclusion follows directly: (1) universal affirmative, (2) universal negative, (3) particular affirmative, (4) particular negative.
The author says: 'نتائج هذه الضروب بينة بذاتها لا تحتاج إلى برهان' (the conclusions are self-evident).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الجزئيتان، وهي معتبرة في الصغرى وفي الكبرى، فإذا قرنت إحدى الصغريات الأربع بإحدى الكبريات الأربع يحصل منه ستة عشر ضرباً، لكن اشتراط الأمر الأوّل أسقط ثمانية أضرب " : الصغريان السالبتان مع الكبريات الأربع. والثاني أربعة أخرى : الصغريان الموجبتان مع الجزئيتين، فلم يبق إلا أربعة أضرب : الأول من موجبتين كليتين ينتج موجبة كلية، كقولنا : «كل (ج ب) وكل (با) فكل (ج ) ». الثاني من كلّيتين والصغرى موجبة كلية و الكبرى سالبة كلية ينتج سالبة كقولنا : كلٌ (ج) (ب) ولا شيء من (ب (۱) فلاشيء من (ج )». الثالث من موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا : «بعض (ج ب) وكل (ب (۱) فبعض (ج )». الرابع : من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا «بعض (ج) (ب) ولاشيء من (ب )) فليس بعض (ج 1). ونتائج هذه الضروب بينة بذاتها لا تحتاج إلى برهان. واعلم أن حاصل الشكل الأول هو اندراج الأصغر بكله أو بعضه في الأوسط المحكوم عليه كلّيّاً بالأكبر إيجاباً أو سلباً، فيكون الأصغر بكله أو بعضه أيضاً محكوماً عليه بالأكبر، إما إيجاباً أو سلباً، فينتج المحصورات الأربع وذلك من خواصه، فإن ما عداه لا ينتج إيجاباً كلياً. وإن حاصل الشكل الثاني أن الأصغر والأكبر متنافيان في الأوسط إيجاباً وسلباً، فيتنافيان قطعاً، فيكون الأكبر مسلوباً عن الأصغر كلياً أو جزئياً، فلا ينتج الشكل الثاني إلا سالبة، فضربان منه ينتجان سالبة كلية، وأخران سالبة جزئية. وإن حاصل الشكل الثالث أن الأصغر لاقى الأوسط إيجاباً والأكبر لاقاه إمّا إيجاباً أو سلباً، فيتلاقيان في الجملة إما إيجاباً أو سلباً، فلا ينتج الشكل الثالث إلاّ جزئية، فثلاثة ضروب منه تنتج موجبة جزئية، وثلاثة أخرى سالبة جزئية. وأما الشكل الرابع فينتج موجبة جزئية، وسالبة إما كلية أو جزئية (شريف).
The syllogism [has] two particular premises, which are considered in the minor and major [premises]. So when one of the four minor [premises] is combined with one of the four major [premises], sixteen moods result. But the first condition dropped eight moods: the two negative minor [premises] with the four major [premises]. And the second [dropped] four others: the two affirmative minor [premises] with the two particular [major premises]. So only four moods remain: the first from two universal affirmatives produces a universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every J is B and every B is A, so every J is A.' The second from two universals, with the minor universal affirmative and the major universal negative, produces a universal negative, as we say: 'Every J is B and no B is A, so no J is A.' The third from two affirmatives, with the minor particular, produces a particular affirmative, as we say: 'Some J is B and every B is A, so some J is A.' The fourth from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative, as we say: 'Some J is B and no B is A, so some J is not A.' The conclusions of these moods are self-evident and need no proof. And know that the upshot of the first figure is that the minor [term] is wholly or partially contained in the middle [term] which is judged universally by the major [term] affirmatively or negatively, so the minor is also wholly or partially judged by the major, either affirmatively or negatively, thus it produces all four categorical propositions; this is one of its properties, for other than it does not produce a universal affirmative. And the upshot of the second figure is that the minor and major [terms] are opposed in the middle [term] affirmatively and negatively, so they are absolutely opposed, thus the major is denied from the minor universally or particularly, so the second figure only produces a negative; two of its moods produce a universal negative, and two a particular negative. And the upshot of the third figure is that the minor meets the middle affirmatively and the major meets it either affirmatively or negatively, so they meet in some respect either affirmatively or negatively, thus the third figure only produces a particular; three of its moods produce a particular affirmative, and three a particular negative. As for the fourth figure, it produces a particular affirmative and a negative, either universal or particular (Sharif).
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the valid moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism, followed by a characterization of all four figures. The author begins by noting that there are two types of premises (minor and major) and each can be one of four based on quantity and quality: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Combining all four minors with all four majors yields sixteen possible moods. However, two conditions reduce these to four valid moods in the first figure: first, the minor cannot be negative (so the two negative minors are dropped, removing eight moods), and second, if the minor is affirmative, the major must be universal (so the two affirmative minors combined with the two particular majors are dropped, removing four more). Thus only four moods remain, and the author lists them with examples using the variables J, B, A to represent the minor, middle, and major terms respectively. The moods are: (1) universal affirmative conclusion from two universal affirmatives; (2) universal negative from universal affirmative minor and universal negative major; (3) particular affirmative from particular affirmative minor and universal affirmative major; (4) particular negative from particular affirmative minor and universal negative major. The author then explains that the conclusions of these moods are self-evident, meaning they do not need further proof. Next, the author describes the essential nature of each figure. For the first figure, the minor term falls under the middle term, which is universally predicated of it, and the middle is also subject to the major term, so the minor inherits the judgment of the major. This allows the first figure to produce all four categorical propositions, and it is the only figure that yields a universal affirmative. For the second figure, the minor and major terms are both related to the middle but in opposite ways (one affirmative, one negative), leading to a negative conclusion. Thus the second figure only yields negative conclusions (universal or particular). For the third figure, both minor and major are related to the middle affirmatively at least partially, so the conclusion is always particular (affirmative or negative). For the fourth figure, it can yield a particular affirmative and a negative that may be universal or particular. These descriptions highlight the unique logical properties of each figure, which are foundational for constructing syllogisms in Islamic logic.

مقدمة صغرى
مقدمة كبرى
موجبة كلية
سالبة كلية
موجبة جزئية
سالبة جزئية

Key Takeaway
The first figure's four valid moods are the only ones in this figure that satisfy the conditions of affirmative minor and universal major, producing all four categorical propositions.

Characterizing the Four Syllogistic Figures
Author characterizes each of the four syllogistic figures by their conclusion types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the distinctive features of each syllogistic figure: what types of conclusions each figure can produce and why.

Core Idea
Each figure has unique properties: first produces all, second only negatives, third only particulars, fourth a mix.

After listing the moods of the first figure, the author characterizes each of the four figures based on the relation of the terms. These characterizations explain why each figure can only yield certain types of conclusions, which is crucial for selecting the right figure in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the first figure's nature
The minor term is wholly or partially contained in the middle term, which is universally judged by the major term. This allows all four conclusion types.
The passage states: 'حاصل الشكل الأول هو اندراج الأصغر بكله أو بعضه في الأوسط المحكوم عليه كلّيّاً بالأكبر'.
2
Understand the second figure
The minor and major terms are opposed in the middle term (one affirmative, one negative), so the conclusion is always negative (universal or particular).
Passage: 'الأصغر والأكبر متنافيان في الأوسط إيجاباً وسلباً، فيتنافيان قطعاً'.
3
Analyze the third figure
The minor term meets the middle affirmatively, and the major meets it either affirmatively or negatively, so the conclusion is always particular (affirmative or negative).
Passage: 'الأصغر لاقى الأوسط إيجاباً والأكبر لاقاه إمّا إيجاباً أو سلباً، فيتلاقيان في الجملة'.
4
Note the fourth figure
The fourth figure produces a particular affirmative and a negative that can be universal or particular.
Passage: 'الشكل الرابع فينتج موجبة جزئية، وسالبة إما كلية أو جزئية'.

Source Text

The syllogism [has] two particular premises, which are considered in the minor and major [premises]. So when one of the four minor [premises] is combined with one of the four major [premises], sixteen moods result. But the first condition dropped eight moods: the two negative minor [premises] with the four major [premises]. And the second [dropped] four others: the two affirmative minor [premises] with the two particular [major premises]. So only four moods remain: the first from two universal affirmatives produces a universal affirmative, as we say: 'Every J is B and every B is A, so every J is A.' The second from two universals, with the minor universal affirmative and the major universal negative, produces a universal negative, as we say: 'Every J is B and no B is A, so no J is A.' The third from two affirmatives, with the minor particular, produces a particular affirmative, as we say: 'Some J is B and every B is A, so some J is A.' The fourth from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative, as we say: 'Some J is B and no B is A, so some J is not A.' The conclusions of these moods are self-evident and need no proof. And know that the upshot of the first figure is that the minor [term] is wholly or partially contained in the middle [term] which is judged universally by the major [term] affirmatively or negatively, so the minor is also wholly or partially judged by the major, either affirmatively or negatively, thus it produces all four categorical propositions; this is one of its properties, for other than it does not produce a universal affirmative. And the upshot of the second figure is that the minor and major [terms] are opposed in the middle [term] affirmatively and negatively, so they are absolutely opposed, thus the major is denied from the minor universally or particularly, so the second figure only produces a negative; two of its moods produce a universal negative, and two a particular negative. The Syllogism

Moods of the First Figure: Celarent, Darii, Ferio
Author lists second, third, and fourth moods of first figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the second, third, and fourth moods of the first figure and verify their validity.

Core Idea
The first figure's moods produce self-evident conclusions from premises with specific quantity and quality.

You already know the first mood (Barbara) from the first figure. Now the author completes the picture with three more moods: Celarent, Darii, and Ferio. Each has a distinct pattern of premises that yields a self-evident conclusion. Pay close attention to the examples, as they illustrate how to map any categorical syllogism onto these forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood's premises and conclusion
Find the minor premise (with subject J), major premise (with predicate A), and conclusion in the example. For instance, in 'Every J is B, and no B is A, so no J is A,' the minor is 'Every J is B,' the major is 'no B is A,' and the conclusion is 'no J is A.'
2
Determine the quality of each premise
Note whether each premise is affirmative (ījāb) or negative (salab). In Celarent, the minor is affirmative, the major is negative. In Darii, both are affirmative. In Ferio, the minor is affirmative, the major is negative.
Quality is one of two kayfiyyāt.
3
Determine the quantity of each premise
Note whether each premise is universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz’iyya). In Celarent, both are universal. In Darii, the minor is particular, the major is universal. In Ferio, the minor is particular, the major is universal.
Quantity is one of two kamiyyāt.
4
Verify the mood matches a productive pattern
Check that the combination of premises matches one of the four valid moods of the first figure: (1) Barbara: AA (universal affirmative + universal affirmative); (2) Celarent: EA (universal negative + universal affirmative); (3) Darii: AII (universal affirmative + particular affirmative); (4) Ferio: EIO (universal negative + particular affirmative).
The author calls them second, third, and fourth moods, with Barbara being first.
5
Confirm the conclusion follows
The conclusion must reflect the quality and quantity dictated by the mood. For Celarent: universal negative; Darii: particular affirmative; Ferio: particular negative. The author says these conclusions are self-evident (bayyin bi-dhātihā).
Self-evident means they require no further proof.

الثاني من كلّيتين والصغرى موجبة كلية و الكبرى سالبة كلية ينتج سالبة كقولنا : كلٌ (ج) (ب) ولا شيء من (ب) [؟] فلاشيء من (ج). الثالث من موجبتين والصغرى جزئية، ينتج موجبة جزئية، كقولنا : «بعض (ج) (ب) وكل (ب) (أ) فبعض (ج)». الرابع : من موجبة جزئية صغرى وسالبة كلية كبرى، ينتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا «بعض (ج) (ب) ولاشيء من (ب) (أ) فليس بعض (ج)». ونتائج هذه الضروب بينة بذاتها لا تحتاج إلى برهان. واعلم أن هاهنا كيفيتين إيجاباً وسلباً - و أشرفها الإيجاب، لأنه وجود والسلب عدم، والوجود أشرف - وكميتين : الكلية والجزئية، وأشرفهما الكلية، لأنه أضبط وأنفع في العلوم وأخص من الجزئية، والأخص لاشتماله على أمر زائد أشرف. فعلى هذا تكون الموجبة الكلّيّة أشرف المحصورات لاشتمالها على أشرفين. وأخسها السالبة الجزئية، لاحتوائها على أخسين. والسالبة الكلية أشرف من الموجبة الجزئية، لأن شرف السلب الكلي باعتبار الكلية، و شرف الإيجاب الجزئي بحسب الإيجاب، وشرف الإيجاب من جهة واحدة وشرف الكلية من جهات متعدّدة. ولما كان المقصود من الأقيسة نتائجها رتبت باعتبار ترتیب نتائجها شرفاً، فقدم المنتج للأشرف على غيره.
The second [mood] from two universals, with the minor affirmative universal and the major negative universal, produces a negative universal, as in our saying: 'Every J is B, and no B is A, so no J is A.' The third [mood] from two affirmatives, with the minor particular, produces a particular affirmative, as in our saying: 'Some J is B, and every B is A, so some J is A.' The fourth [mood] from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major produces a particular negative, as in our saying: 'Some J is B, and no B is A, so some J is not A.' The conclusions of these moods are self-evident and do not require proof. Know that here there are two qualities: affirmation and negation – the noblest of them is affirmation, because it is existence and negation is non-existence, and existence is nobler – and two quantities: universality and particularity, the noblest of which is universality, because it is more precise, more useful in the sciences, and more specific than particularity, and the more specific is nobler due to its inclusion of an additional attribute. Hence, the universal affirmative is the noblest of the quantified [propositions] because it combines the two nobler aspects. The basest is the particular negative, because it combines the two baser aspects. The universal negative is nobler than the particular affirmative, because the nobility of the universal negative is due to its universality, and the nobility of the particular affirmative is due to its affirmation, and the nobility of affirmation comes from one aspect, while the nobility of universality comes from multiple aspects. Since the purpose of syllogisms is their conclusions, they were ordered according to the nobility of their conclusions, so the one producing the noblest [conclusion] was placed before others.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage completes the enumeration of the four productive moods of the first figure (Barbara was presumably earlier). It gives the second mood (Celarent), third (Darii), and fourth (Ferio) with examples in logical variables. It then asserts that their conclusions are self-evident (bayyin bi-dhatihā). The author then shifts to a discussion of the relative nobility of propositions based on quality (kayfiyya: affirmation/negation) and quantity (kamiyya: universal/particular). Affirmation is nobler than negation because it indicates existence, which is nobler than non-existence. Universality is nobler than particularity because it is more precise and useful in the sciences, and because the universal includes an additional attribute (i.e., applicability to all individuals), making it more specific in meaning. Consequently, the universal affirmative (mūjiba kulliyya) is the noblest, combining the nobler of both dimensions. The particular negative (sāliba juz’iyya) is the basest, combining the baser of both. The universal negative is nobler than the particular affirmative because its nobility comes from universality, which has multiple aspects, while the nobility of affirmation is from one aspect. Finally, the moods of the first figure are arranged in order of the nobility of their conclusions, so the mood yielding the noblest conclusion (the universal affirmative, Barbara) is placed first, though it was not mentioned here, and then Celarent, Darii, Ferio in descending order.

B) Celarent

Key Takeaway
The first figure has four valid moods; each yields a conclusion that is self-evident from the premises.

Ranking Propositions: The Nobility of Universal Affirmatives
Author ranks propositions by nobility based on quality and quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank any categorical proposition in terms of nobility based on its quality and quantity, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Affirmation and universality are nobler than negation and particularity, making universal affirmatives the noblest propositions.

After listing the moods, the author turns to a qualitative ranking of propositions. This may seem like a side topic, but it has real implications for how jurists and theologians weigh evidence. The hierarchy is based on two dimensions: quality (affirmation vs. negation) and quantity (universal vs. particular). Understand this hierarchy, and you will grasp why some arguments carry more weight than others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality of the proposition
Determine whether it is affirmative (ījāb) or negative (salab). The author says affirmation is nobler because it indicates existence, and existence is nobler than non-existence.
2
Identify the quantity of the proposition
Determine whether it is universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz’iyya). The author says universality is nobler because it is more precise, more useful in sciences, and more specific (a‘amm? actually 'more specific' in meaning? He says 'more specific' but likely means 'more encompassing' – check comment: 'more specific' because it includes an additional attribute, i.e., applicability to all individuals).
The term 'akhass' here means more specific in the sense of having a more particular meaning? Actually the author says: 'because it is more precise, more useful in the sciences, and more specific than particularity, and the more specific is nobler due to its inclusion of an additional attribute.' This is a bit tricky: universality is considered 'more specific' (akhass) in the sense that it has a more restricted meaning? Or does he mean 'more comprehensive'? In classical logic, universality is considered more comprehensive but also more precise. We'll follow the text.
3
Apply the hierarchy: affirmation > negation, universal > particular
Combine the judgments: the noblest is universal affirmative (combines nobler of both). The basest is particular negative (combines baser of both). For the mixed cases: universal negative vs. particular affirmative, the author argues universal negative is nobler because its nobility stems from universality, which has multiple aspects, while affirmation's nobility is from a single aspect.
4
Practice ranking the four types
List the four quantified propositions in order from noblest to basest: (1) universal affirmative, (2) universal negative, (3) particular affirmative, (4) particular negative. Check that your order matches the author's reasoning.

Conditions for the Second Figure's Validity
Author states the two conditions for the second figure and shows why violation causes non-production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions (ikhtilāf al-kayf and kulliyyat al-kubrā) for a valid second-figure categorical syllogism and demonstrate why violating either condition renders the syllogism non-productive.

Core Idea
The second figure requires differing quality and a universal major to avoid contradiction.

Every logical figure has its own rules. The second figure's conditions are stricter than the first: not only must the major be universal, but also the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). This passage from Qutbi's commentary explains these conditions and shows why neglecting them leads to a syllogism that can yield both an affirmative and a negative conclusion, making it useless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find in the Arabic text the two conditions stated: 'ikhtilāf al-muqaddimatayn bi al-kayf' and 'kulliyyat al-kubrā'. Note that these are the requirements for a productive second figure.
The passage explicitly says: 'shartuhu ikhtilāf muqaddimatayhi bi al-kayf wa kulliyyat al-kubrā'.
2
Understand why differing quality is needed
Read the commentary (أقول) that explains: if both premises are affirmative, you can find a substitution of terms that yields a true affirmative conclusion and another substitution that yields a true negative conclusion. This inconsistency shows no necessary conclusion.
Example given: 'Every human is an animal and every rational is an animal' gives affirmative; but changing the major premise to 'Every horse is an animal' gives false? Actually the text says 'the truth would be negation' meaning a negative conclusion would be true. The point is the form itself does not force one outcome.
3
Check the universal major condition
The second condition is that the major premise must be universal. The passage implies that if it were particular, again contradictory results could occur. However, the passage only explicitly discusses the quality condition and assumes the quantity condition is similarly necessary.
The commentary says 'wa illā laḥaṣala al-ikhtilāf al-mūjib li 'adam al-intāj' meaning without either condition, difference occurs.
4
Apply to real syllogisms
Now that you know the conditions, test a given second-figure syllogism: are the premises different in quality? Is the major universal? If yes, it may be productive; if no, it is non-productive.
Remember that the second figure's mood must also satisfy these conditions; the valid moods are Cesare, Camestres, Festino, Baroco.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الثاني: شروط إنتاجه وضروبه الناتجة

قال: وأما الشكل الثاني: فشرطه اختلاف مقدمتية بالكيف وكلّية الكبرى، وإلا لحصل الاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج، وهو صدق القياس مع إيجاب النتيجة تارة ومع سلبها أخرى.

أقول: لإنتاج الشكل الثاني أيضاً شرطان بحسب الكيفية والكمية : أما بحسب الكيفية فاختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف، بأن تكون إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة. وأما بحسب الكمية فكلّية الكبرى، وذلك لأنه لولم يتحقق أحد الشرطين لحصل الاختلاف الموجب لعدم الإنتاج، وهو صدق القياس تارة مع الإيجاب وأخرى مع السلب، والاختلاف موجب للعقم. أما لزوم الاختلاف على تقدير انتفاء الشرط الأول : فلانه لو اتفقت المقدمتان في الكيف، فإما أن يكونا موجبتين أو سالبتين، وأيا ما كان يتحقق الاختلاف :
أما إذا كانتا موجبتين : فلأنه يصدق «كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق حيوان» والحق الإيجاب - ولويد لنا الكبرى بقولنا : «وكل فرس حيوان» كان الحق السلب.
وأما إذا كانتا سالبتين : فلصدق قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولاشيء من الفرس بحجر » - فالحق السلب - ولو قلنا : «ولاشيء من الناطق بحجر» فالحق الإيجاب
The Second Figure: Conditions for Its Production and Its Productive Moods.

He said: As for the second figure, its condition is the difference of its two premises in quality and the universality of the major, otherwise the difference that necessitates non-production would occur, namely the syllogism being true sometimes with affirmation of the conclusion and other times with its negation.

I say: For the production of the second figure there are also two conditions in terms of quality and quantity: As for quality, it is the difference of its two premises in quality, such that one is affirmative and the other negative. As for quantity, it is the universality of the major. This is because if one of the two conditions is not realized, the difference that necessitates non-production would occur, namely the syllogism being true sometimes with affirmation and other times with negation, and difference leads to sterility. As for the necessity of difference on the assumption that the first condition is negated: because if the two premises agree in quality, then either they are both affirmative or both negative, and in either case difference occurs: If they are both affirmative, because [the syllogism] 'Every human is an animal and every rational is an animal' is true – so the truth is affirmation – and if we were to [replace?] the major with our saying 'Every horse is an animal', then the truth would be negation. And if they are both negative, because our saying 'No human is a stone, and no horse is a stone' is true – so the truth is negation – and if we said 'No rational is a stone', then the truth would be affirmation.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in the second figure. The matn states two conditions: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. If either condition is violated, the syllogism becomes non-productive (i.e., does not yield a necessary conclusion). The commentary (أقول) explains why: if both premises are affirmative, we can construct a syllogism like 'Every human is an animal and every rational is an animal' which yields an affirmative conclusion (so the conclusion could be true as affirmative), but if we replace the middle term with 'horse', we get 'Every human is an animal and every horse is an animal' which yields no conclusion (or a false one) – actually the text says 'the truth would be negation', meaning the correct conclusion would be negative? The example is a bit confusing: they are showing that from two affirmatives in the second figure, you can get both an affirmative and a negative conclusion by changing terms, so the form is invalid. Similarly, with two negatives. The key is that without differing quality, the middle term does not fix a determinate relation between subject and predicate. The commentary uses concrete examples from everyday objects (human, animal, rational, horse, stone) to illustrate the point. Note that these are not standard Islamic science examples but are neutral examples; we can relate them to the tradition but the commentary must stay within the text.

كلية الكبرى

C) Because the major must be universal.
D) Because the minor must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The second figure requires both differing quality and a universal major premise; without these, the syllogism cannot guarantee a unique conclusion.

The Syllogism

Conditions for Second Figure Syllogism Production
Author specifies two conditions for second figure production and justifies with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid second figure categorical syllogism and verify whether a given second figure mood satisfies them.

Core Idea
Second figure requires difference in quality and a universal major to avoid sterile moods.

In this passage, the author lays out the precise conditions that a second figure syllogism must meet to produce a determinate conclusion. Without these conditions, the same syllogistic form can yield both a true affirmative and a true negative conclusion under different substitutions — a defect called 'difference' (ikhtilāf) that renders the form unproductive. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating categorical arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find in the passage the two stated conditions: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal.
These conditions are stated explicitly at the beginning.
2
Examine the first counterexample: both premises affirmative
The author shows that if both premises are affirmative, substituting different terms yields a true affirmative conclusion in one case and a true negative in another. This demonstrates that the mood does not uniquely determine the conclusion's quality.
Example: 'Every human is animal, every rational is animal' vs. 'every horse is animal' as major.
3
Examine the second counterexample: both premises negative
Similarly, if both premises are negative, different substitutions give both true negatives and true affirmatives, so the mood is sterile.
Example: 'Nothing human is stone, nothing horse is stone' vs. 'nothing rational is stone'.
4
Examine the counterexample: particular major premise
If the major premise is particular (affirmative or negative), again different substitutions yield both true affirmatives and negatives, showing the necessity of a universal major.
Example with particular affirmative: 'Nothing human is horse, some animal is horse' yields true affirmative; substituting 'some whinnying is horse' yields true negative.
5
Synthesize the lesson
The key insight: for a syllogism to be productive, its form must entail exactly one determinate conclusion. If the same mood can produce both a true affirmative and a true negative conclusion under different term substitutions, it is sterile.
